<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<p>The <b>Mississippi River</b> is the chief river of the second-largest <a href="/wiki/Drainage_system_(geomorphology)" title="Drainage system (geomorphology)">drainage system</a> on the North American continent, second only to the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a> drainage system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSlongest_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGSlongest-11">[11]</a></sup> Flowing entirely in the United States (although its <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> reaches into Canada), it rises in northern <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meander" title="Meander">meanders</a> slowly southwards for 2,320 miles (3,730 km)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSlongest_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGSlongest-11">[11]</a></sup> to the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River_Delta" title="Mississippi River Delta">Mississippi River Delta</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>. With its many <a href="/wiki/Tributary" title="Tributary">tributaries</a>, the Mississippi's <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">watershed</a> drains all or parts of 31 <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a> and 2 <a href="/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada" title="Provinces and territories of Canada">Canadian provinces</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">Appalachian Mountains</a>. The Mississippi ranks as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_by_length" title="List of rivers by length">fourth-longest</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_by_discharge" title="List of rivers by discharge">fifteenth-largest</a> <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river</a> in the world by discharge. The river either borders or passes through the states of <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a> long lived along the Mississippi River and its tributaries. Most were <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a>, but some, such as the <a href="/wiki/Mound_Builders" title="Mound Builders">Mound Builders</a>, formed prolific agricultural societies. The arrival of Europeans in the 16th century changed the native way of life as first explorers, then settlers, ventured into the basin in increasing numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The river served first as a barrier, forming borders for <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">New Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">New France</a>, and the early United States, and then as a vital transportation artery and communications link. In the 19th century, during the height of the ideology of <a href="/wiki/Manifest_destiny" title="Manifest destiny">manifest destiny</a>, the Mississippi and several western tributaries, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_River" title="Missouri River">Missouri</a>, formed pathways for the western expansion of the United States.</p>
<p>Formed from thick layers of the river's <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a> deposits, the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_embayment" title="Mississippi embayment">Mississippi embayment</a> is one of the most fertile agricultural regions of the country, which resulted in the river's storied <a href="/wiki/Steamboats_of_the_Mississippi" title="Steamboats of the Mississippi">steamboat</a> era. During the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, the Mississippi's capture by <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> forces marked a turning point towards victory due to the river's importance as a route of trade and travel, not least to the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a>. Because of substantial growth of cities and the larger ships and <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">barges</a> that supplanted riverboats, the first decades of the 20th century saw the construction of massive <a href="/wiki/River_engineering" title="River engineering">engineering works</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">levees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lock_(water_navigation)" title="Lock (water navigation)">locks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dams</a>, often built in combination.</p>
<p>Since modern development of the basin began, the Mississippi has also seen its share of pollution and environmental problems – most notably large volumes of agricultural runoff, which has led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico_dead_zone" title="Gulf of Mexico dead zone">Gulf of Mexico dead zone</a> off the Delta. In recent years, the river has shown a steady shift towards the <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_River" title="Atchafalaya River">Atchafalaya River</a> channel in the Delta; a course change would be an economic disaster for the port city of <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_significance">Name and significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name and significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word itself comes from <i>Messipi</i>, the French rendering of the <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe_language" title="Ojibwe language">Anishinaabe</a> (Ojibwe or Algonquin) name for the river, <i>Misi-ziibi</i> (Great River).</p>
<p>In the 18th century, the river was the primary western boundary of the young United States, and since the country's expansion westward, the Mississippi River has been widely considered a convenient if approximate dividing line between the Eastern, Southern, and Midwestern United States, and the Western United States. This is exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/Gateway_Arch" title="Gateway Arch">Gateway Arch</a> in St. Louis and the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Trans-Mississippi" title="Trans-Mississippi">Trans-Mississippi</a>" as used in the name of the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Mississippi_Exposition" title="Trans-Mississippi Exposition">Trans-Mississippi Exposition</a>.</p>
<p>It is common to qualify a regionally superlative landmark in relation to it, such as "the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Mitchell" title="Mount Mitchell">highest peak east of the Mississippi</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> or "the oldest city west of the Mississippi".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">FCC</a> also uses it as the dividing line for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broadcast_callsign" title="Broadcast callsign">broadcast callsigns</a>, which begin with W to the east and K to the west, mixing together in <a href="/wiki/Media_market" title="Media market">media markets</a> along the river.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_geography">Physical geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The geographical setting of the Mississippi River includes considerations of the course of the river itself, its watershed, its outflow, its prehistoric and historic course changes, and possibilities of future course changes. The <a href="/wiki/New_Madrid_Seismic_Zone" title="New Madrid Seismic Zone">New Madrid Seismic Zone</a> along the river is also noteworthy. These various basic geographical aspects of the river in turn underlie its human history and present uses of the waterway and its adjacent lands.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Divisions">Divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Mississippi River can be divided into three sections: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Mississippi" title="Upper Mississippi">Upper Mississippi</a>, the river from its headwaters to the confluence with the Missouri River; the Middle Mississippi, which is downriver from the Missouri to the Ohio River; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_Mississippi" title="Lower Mississippi">Lower Mississippi</a>, which flows from the Ohio to the Gulf of Mexico.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Upper_Mississippi">Upper Mississippi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Upper Mississippi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lake_Itasca_Mississippi_Source.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Lake_Itasca_Mississippi_Source.jpg/220px-Lake_Itasca_Mississippi_Source.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Lake_Itasca_Mississippi_Source.jpg/330px-Lake_Itasca_Mississippi_Source.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Lake_Itasca_Mississippi_Source.jpg/440px-Lake_Itasca_Mississippi_Source.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lake_Itasca_Mississippi_Source.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The beginning of the Mississippi River at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Itasca" title="Lake Itasca">Lake Itasca</a> (2004)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Anthony_Falls_aerial.jpg"><img alt="St. Anthony Falls" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="452" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Saint_Anthony_Falls_aerial.jpg/220px-Saint_Anthony_Falls_aerial.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Saint_Anthony_Falls_aerial.jpg/330px-Saint_Anthony_Falls_aerial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Saint_Anthony_Falls_aerial.jpg/440px-Saint_Anthony_Falls_aerial.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Anthony_Falls_aerial.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mississippi head of navigation, St. Anthony Falls</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WyalusingStateParkWisconsinRiverIntoMississippiRiver.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2288" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/WyalusingStateParkWisconsinRiverIntoMississippiRiver.jpg/220px-WyalusingStateParkWisconsinRiverIntoMississippiRiver.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/WyalusingStateParkWisconsinRiverIntoMississippiRiver.jpg/330px-WyalusingStateParkWisconsinRiverIntoMississippiRiver.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/WyalusingStateParkWisconsinRiverIntoMississippiRiver.jpg/440px-WyalusingStateParkWisconsinRiverIntoMississippiRiver.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WyalusingStateParkWisconsinRiverIntoMississippiRiver.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Confluence of the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_River" title="Wisconsin River">Wisconsin</a> and Mississippi Rivers, viewed from <a href="/wiki/Wyalusing_State_Park" title="Wyalusing State Park">Wyalusing State Park</a> in Wisconsin</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Upper Mississippi runs from its headwaters to its confluence with the Missouri River at St. Louis, Missouri. It is divided into two sections:</p>
<ol>
<li>The headwaters, 493 miles (793 km) from the source to <a href="/wiki/Saint_Anthony_Falls" title="Saint Anthony Falls">Saint Anthony Falls</a> in <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis" title="Minneapolis">Minneapolis, Minnesota</a>; and</li>
<li>A navigable channel, formed by a series of man-made lakes between Minneapolis and St. Louis, Missouri, some 664 miles (1,069 km).</li>
</ol>
<p>The source of the Upper Mississippi branch is traditionally accepted as <a href="/wiki/Lake_Itasca" title="Lake Itasca">Lake Itasca</a>, 1,475 feet (450 m) above sea level in <a href="/wiki/Itasca_State_Park" title="Itasca State Park">Itasca State Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Clearwater_County,_Minnesota" title="Clearwater County, Minnesota">Clearwater County, Minnesota</a>. The name "Itasca" was chosen to designate the "true head" of the Mississippi River as a combination of the last four letters of the Latin word for truth (<i>veritas</i>) and the first two letters of the Latin word for head (<i>caput</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> However, the lake is in turn fed by a number of smaller streams.</p>
<p>From its origin at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Itasca" title="Lake Itasca">Lake Itasca</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis, Missouri</a>, the waterway's flow is moderated by 43 dams. Fourteen of these dams are located above Minneapolis in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Headwater" title="Headwater">headwaters</a> region and serve multiple purposes, including power generation and recreation. The remaining 29 dams, beginning in downtown Minneapolis, all contain locks and were constructed to improve commercial navigation of the upper river. Taken as a whole, these 43 dams significantly shape the geography and influence the ecology of the upper river. Beginning just below <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul,_Minnesota" title="Saint Paul, Minnesota">Saint Paul, Minnesota</a>, and continuing throughout the upper and lower river, the Mississippi is further controlled by thousands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wing_Dike" title="Wing Dike">wing dikes</a> that moderate the river's flow in order to maintain an open navigation channel and prevent the river from eroding its banks.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Head_of_navigation" title="Head of navigation">head of navigation</a> on the Mississippi is the Coon Rapids Dam in <a href="/wiki/Coon_Rapids,_Minnesota" title="Coon Rapids, Minnesota">Coon Rapids, Minnesota</a>. Before it was built in 1913, steamboats could occasionally go upstream as far as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Cloud,_Minnesota" title="Saint Cloud, Minnesota">Saint Cloud, Minnesota</a>, depending on river conditions.</p>
<p>The uppermost lock and dam on the Upper Mississippi River is the Upper St. Anthony Falls Lock and Dam in Minneapolis. Above the dam, the river's elevation is 799 feet (244 m). Below the dam, the river's elevation is 750 feet (230 m). This 49-foot (15 m) drop is the largest of all the Mississippi River locks and dams. The origin of the dramatic drop is a waterfall preserved adjacent to the lock under an apron of concrete. <a href="/wiki/Saint_Anthony_Falls" title="Saint Anthony Falls">Saint Anthony Falls</a> is the only true waterfall on the entire Mississippi River. The water elevation continues to drop steeply as it passes through the gorge carved by the waterfall.</p>
<p>After the completion of the St. Anthony Falls Lock and Dam in 1963, the river's head of navigation moved upstream, to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coon_Rapids_Dam" title="Coon Rapids Dam">Coon Rapids Dam</a>. However, the Locks were closed in 2015 to control the spread of invasive <a href="/wiki/Asian_carp_in_North_America" title="Asian carp in North America">Asian carp</a>, making Minneapolis once again the site of the head of navigation of the river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Upper Mississippi has a number of natural and artificial lakes, with its widest point being <a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnibigoshish" title="Lake Winnibigoshish">Lake Winnibigoshish</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Grand_Rapids,_Minnesota" title="Grand Rapids, Minnesota">Grand Rapids, Minnesota</a>, over 11 miles (18 km) across. <a href="/wiki/Lake_Onalaska" title="Lake Onalaska">Lake Onalaska</a>, created by <a href="/wiki/Lock_and_Dam_No._7" title="Lock and Dam No. 7">Lock and Dam No. 7</a>, near <a href="/wiki/La_Crosse,_Wisconsin" title="La Crosse, Wisconsin">La Crosse, Wisconsin</a>, is more than 4 miles (6.4 km) wide. <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pepin" title="Lake Pepin">Lake Pepin</a>, a natural lake formed behind the delta of the <a href="/wiki/Chippewa_River_(Wisconsin)" title="Chippewa River (Wisconsin)">Chippewa River</a> of Wisconsin as it enters the Upper Mississippi, is more than 2 miles (3.2 km) wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the time the Upper Mississippi reaches <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul,_Minnesota" title="Saint Paul, Minnesota">Saint Paul</a>, Minnesota, below Lock and Dam No. 1, it has dropped more than half its original elevation and is 687 feet (209 m) above sea level. From St. Paul to St. Louis, Missouri, the river elevation falls much more slowly, and is controlled and managed as a series of pools created by 26 locks and dams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Upper Mississippi River is joined by the <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_River" title="Minnesota River">Minnesota River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Snelling" title="Fort Snelling">Fort Snelling</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis%E2%80%93Saint_Paul" title="Minneapolis–Saint Paul">Twin Cities</a>; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Croix_River_(Wisconsin-Minnesota)" title="St. Croix River (Wisconsin-Minnesota)">St. Croix River</a> near <a href="/wiki/Prescott,_Wisconsin" title="Prescott, Wisconsin">Prescott, Wisconsin</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Cannon_River_(Minnesota)" title="Cannon River (Minnesota)">Cannon River</a> near <a href="/wiki/Red_Wing,_Minnesota" title="Red Wing, Minnesota">Red Wing, Minnesota</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Zumbro_River" title="Zumbro River">Zumbro River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Wabasha,_Minnesota" title="Wabasha, Minnesota">Wabasha, Minnesota</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Black_River_(Wisconsin)" title="Black River (Wisconsin)">Black</a>, <a href="/wiki/La_Crosse_River" title="La Crosse River">La Crosse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Root_River_(Minnesota)" title="Root River (Minnesota)">Root</a> rivers in <a href="/wiki/La_Crosse,_Wisconsin" title="La Crosse, Wisconsin">La Crosse, Wisconsin</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_River" title="Wisconsin River">Wisconsin River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Prairie_du_Chien,_Wisconsin" title="Prairie du Chien, Wisconsin">Prairie du Chien, Wisconsin</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Rock_River_(Mississippi_River)" title="Rock River (Mississippi River)">Rock River</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Quad_Cities" title="Quad Cities">Quad Cities</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Iowa_River" title="Iowa River">Iowa River</a> near <a href="/wiki/Wapello,_Iowa" title="Wapello, Iowa">Wapello, Iowa</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Skunk_River" title="Skunk River">Skunk River</a> south of <a href="/wiki/Burlington,_Iowa" title="Burlington, Iowa">Burlington, Iowa</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Des_Moines_River" title="Des Moines River">Des Moines River</a> at Keokuk, Iowa. Other major tributaries of the Upper Mississippi include the <a href="/wiki/Crow_River_(Minnesota)" title="Crow River (Minnesota)">Crow River</a> in Minnesota, the <a href="/wiki/Chippewa_River_(Wisconsin)" title="Chippewa River (Wisconsin)">Chippewa River</a> in Wisconsin, the <a href="/wiki/Maquoketa_River" title="Maquoketa River">Maquoketa River</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Wapsipinicon_River" title="Wapsipinicon River">Wapsipinicon River</a> in Iowa, and the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_River" title="Illinois River">Illinois River</a> in Illinois.</p>
<p>The Upper Mississippi is largely a multi-thread stream with many <a href="/wiki/Bar_(river_morphology)" title="Bar (river morphology)">bars</a> and islands. From its confluence with the St. Croix River downstream to <a href="/wiki/Dubuque,_Iowa" title="Dubuque, Iowa">Dubuque, Iowa</a>, the river is entrenched, with high bedrock bluffs lying on either side. The height of these bluffs decreases to the south of Dubuque, though they are still significant through <a href="/wiki/Savanna,_Illinois" title="Savanna, Illinois">Savanna, Illinois</a>. This topography contrasts strongly with the Lower Mississippi, which is a meandering river in a broad, flat area, only rarely flowing alongside a bluff (as at <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg,_Mississippi" title="Vicksburg, Mississippi">Vicksburg, Mississippi</a>).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CairoIL_from_space_annotated.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/CairoIL_from_space_annotated.jpg/220px-CairoIL_from_space_annotated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/CairoIL_from_space_annotated.jpg/330px-CairoIL_from_space_annotated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/CairoIL_from_space_annotated.jpg/440px-CairoIL_from_space_annotated.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CairoIL_from_space_annotated.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Confluence" title="Confluence">confluence</a> of the Mississippi (left) and Ohio (right) rivers at <a href="/wiki/Cairo,_Illinois" title="Cairo, Illinois">Cairo</a>, Illinois, the demarcation between the Middle and the Lower Mississippi River</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Mississippi">Middle Mississippi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Middle Mississippi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Mississippi River is known as the Middle Mississippi from the Upper Mississippi River's confluence with the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_River" title="Missouri River">Missouri River</a> at St. Louis, Missouri, for 190 miles (310 km) to its confluence with the Ohio River at Cairo, Illinois.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corps_Middle_Mississippi_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corps_Middle_Mississippi-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMRP_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMRP-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Middle Mississippi is relatively free-flowing. From St. Louis to the Ohio River confluence, the Middle Mississippi falls 220 feet (67 m) over 180 miles (290 km) for an average rate of 1.2 feet per mile (23 cm/km). At its confluence with the Ohio River, the Middle Mississippi is 315 feet (96 m) above sea level. Apart from the Missouri and <a href="/wiki/Meramec_River" title="Meramec River">Meramec</a> rivers of Missouri and the <a href="/wiki/Kaskaskia_River" title="Kaskaskia River">Kaskaskia River</a> of Illinois, no major tributaries enter the Middle Mississippi River.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lower_Mississippi">Lower Mississippi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Lower Mississippi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_-_New_Orleans.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Mississippi_River_-_New_Orleans.jpg/220px-Mississippi_River_-_New_Orleans.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Mississippi_River_-_New_Orleans.jpg/330px-Mississippi_River_-_New_Orleans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Mississippi_River_-_New_Orleans.jpg/440px-Mississippi_River_-_New_Orleans.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_-_New_Orleans.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lower Mississippi River near New Orleans</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Mississippi River is called the Lower Mississippi River from its confluence with the Ohio River to its mouth at the Gulf of Mexico, a distance of about 1,000 miles (1,600 km). At the confluence of the Ohio and the Middle Mississippi, the long-term mean discharge of the Ohio at Cairo, Illinois is 281,500 cubic feet per second (7,970 cubic metres per second),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> while the long-term mean discharge of the Mississippi at Thebes, Illinois (just upriver from Cairo) is 208,200 cu ft/s (5,900 m<sup>3</sup>/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Thus, by volume, the main branch of the Mississippi River system at Cairo can be considered to be the Ohio River (and the <a href="/wiki/Allegheny_River" title="Allegheny River">Allegheny River</a> further upstream), rather than the Middle Mississippi.</p>
<p>In addition to the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>, the major tributaries of the Lower Mississippi River are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_River_(Arkansas)" title="White River (Arkansas)">White River</a>, flowing in at the <a href="/wiki/White_River_National_Wildlife_Refuge" title="White River National Wildlife Refuge">White River National Wildlife Refuge</a> in east central Arkansas; the <a href="/wiki/Arkansas_River" title="Arkansas River">Arkansas River</a>, joining the Mississippi at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arkansas_Post_National_Memorial" title="Arkansas Post National Memorial">Arkansas Post</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Big_Black_River_(Mississippi)" title="Big Black River (Mississippi)">Big Black River</a> in Mississippi; and the <a href="/wiki/Yazoo_River" title="Yazoo River">Yazoo River</a>, meeting the Mississippi at <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg,_Mississippi" title="Vicksburg, Mississippi">Vicksburg, Mississippi</a>. The widest point of the Mississippi River is in the Lower Mississippi portion where it exceeds 1 mile (1.6 km) in width in several places.</p>
<p>Deliberate water diversion at the <a href="/wiki/Old_River_Control_Structure" title="Old River Control Structure">Old River Control Structure</a> in <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> allows the <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_River" title="Atchafalaya River">Atchafalaya River</a> in Louisiana to be a major <a href="/wiki/Distributary" title="Distributary">distributary</a> of the Mississippi River, with 30% of the Mississippi flowing to the Gulf of Mexico by this route, rather than continuing down the Mississippi's current channel past <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baton_Rouge" title="Baton Rouge">Baton Rouge</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> on a longer route to the Gulf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kemp_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-kemp-27">[27]</a></sup> Although the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_South" title="Red River of the South">Red River</a> is commonly thought to be a tributary, it is actually not, because its water flows separately into the Gulf of Mexico through the Atchafalaya River.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Watershed">Watershed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Watershed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_Watershed_Map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="494" data-file-width="800" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Mississippi_River_Watershed_Map.jpg/220px-Mississippi_River_Watershed_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Mississippi_River_Watershed_Map.jpg/330px-Mississippi_River_Watershed_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Mississippi_River_Watershed_Map.jpg/440px-Mississippi_River_Watershed_Map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_Watershed_Map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the Mississippi River watershed.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Rivers_of_the_Mississippi_Watershed.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An animation of the flows along the rivers of the Mississippi watershed.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_drainage_basins_by_area" title="List of drainage basins by area">List of drainage basins by area</a></div>
<p>The Mississippi River has the world's fourth-largest <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> ("watershed" or "catchment"). The basin covers more than 1,245,000 square miles (3,220,000 km<sup>2</sup>), including all or parts of 31<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> U.S. states and two Canadian provinces. The drainage basin empties into the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, part of the Atlantic Ocean. The total catchment of the Mississippi River covers nearly 40% of the landmass of the continental United States. The highest point within the watershed is also the highest point of the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Elbert" title="Mount Elbert">Mount Elbert</a> at 14,440 feet (4,400 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MississippiRiver_GulfMex_MODIS_2004jul-aug.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1085" data-file-width="540" height="442" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/MississippiRiver_GulfMex_MODIS_2004jul-aug.jpg/220px-MississippiRiver_GulfMex_MODIS_2004jul-aug.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/MississippiRiver_GulfMex_MODIS_2004jul-aug.jpg/330px-MississippiRiver_GulfMex_MODIS_2004jul-aug.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/MississippiRiver_GulfMex_MODIS_2004jul-aug.jpg/440px-MississippiRiver_GulfMex_MODIS_2004jul-aug.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MississippiRiver_GulfMex_MODIS_2004jul-aug.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sequence of NASA <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moderate-Resolution_Imaging_Spectroradiometer" title="Moderate-Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer">MODIS</a> images showing the outflow of fresh water from the Mississippi (arrows) into the Gulf of Mexico (2004)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the United States, the Mississippi River drains the majority of the area between the crest of the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> and the crest of the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">Appalachian Mountains</a>, except for various regions drained to <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_North" title="Red River of the North">Red River of the North</a>; to the Atlantic Ocean by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_River" title="Saint Lawrence River">Saint Lawrence River</a>; and to the Gulf of Mexico by the <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande" title="Rio Grande">Rio Grande</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_River" title="Alabama River">Alabama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tombigbee_River" title="Tombigbee River">Tombigbee</a> rivers, the <a href="/wiki/Chattahoochee_River" title="Chattahoochee River">Chattahoochee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apalachicola_River" title="Apalachicola River">Appalachicola</a> rivers, and various smaller coastal waterways along the Gulf.</p>
<p>The Mississippi River empties into the Gulf of Mexico about 100 miles (160 km) downstream from New Orleans. Measurements of the length of the Mississippi from Lake Itasca to the Gulf of Mexico vary somewhat, but the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a>'s number is 2,320 miles (3,730 km). The retention time from Lake Itasca to the Gulf is typically about 90 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outflow">Outflow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Outflow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Mississippi River discharges at an annual average rate of between 200 and 700 thousand cubic feet per second (7,000–20,000 m<sup>3</sup>/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mississippirivercfs_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-mississippirivercfs-31">[31]</a></sup> Although it is the fifth-largest river in the world by volume, this flow is a small fraction of the output of <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">the Amazon</a>, which moves nearly 7 million cubic feet per second (200,000 m<sup>3</sup>/s) during wet seasons. On average, the Mississippi has only 8% the flow of the Amazon River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fresh river water flowing from the Mississippi into the Gulf of Mexico does not mix into the salt water immediately. The images from <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moderate-Resolution_Imaging_Spectroradiometer" title="Moderate-Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer">MODIS</a> (to the right) show a large plume of fresh water, which appears as a dark ribbon against the lighter-blue surrounding waters. These images demonstrate that the plume did not mix with the surrounding sea water immediately. Instead, it stayed intact as it flowed through the Gulf of Mexico, into the <a href="/wiki/Straits_of_Florida" title="Straits of Florida">Straits of Florida</a>, and entered the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>. The Mississippi River water rounded the tip of Florida and traveled up the southeast coast to the latitude of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> before finally mixing in so thoroughly with the ocean that it could no longer be detected by MODIS.</p>
<p>Before 1900, the Mississippi River transported an estimated 400 million metric tons of <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> per year from the interior of the United States to coastal Louisiana and the Gulf of Mexico. During the last two decades, this number was only 145 million metric tons per year. The reduction in <a href="/wiki/Sediment_transport" title="Sediment transport">sediment transported</a> down the Mississippi River is the result of engineering modification of the Mississippi, Missouri, and Ohio rivers and their tributaries by dams, <a href="/wiki/Meander_cutoff" title="Meander cutoff">meander cutoffs</a>, river-training structures, and bank revetments and soil <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a> control programs in the areas drained by them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MeadelOthers1984_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-MeadelOthers1984-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Course_changes">Course changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Course changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Over geologic time, the Mississippi River has experienced numerous large and small changes to its main course, as well as additions, deletions, and other changes among its numerous tributaries, and the lower Mississippi River has used different pathways as its main channel to the Gulf of Mexico across the delta region.</p>
<p>Through a natural process known as <a href="/wiki/Avulsion_(river)" title="Avulsion (river)">avulsion</a> or delta switching, the lower Mississippi River has shifted its final course to the mouth of the Gulf of Mexico every thousand years or so. This occurs because the deposits of silt and sediment begin to clog its channel, raising the river's level and causing it to eventually find a steeper, more direct route to the Gulf of Mexico. The abandoned distributaries diminish in volume and form what are known as <a href="/wiki/Bayou" title="Bayou">bayous</a>. This process has, over the past 5,000 years, caused the coastline of south Louisiana to advance toward the Gulf from 15 to 50 miles (24 to 80 km). The currently active delta lobe is called the Birdfoot Delta, after its shape, or the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Balize_Delta&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Balize Delta (page does not exist)">Balize Delta</a>, after <a href="/wiki/La_Balize,_Louisiana" title="La Balize, Louisiana">La Balize, Louisiana</a>, the first French settlement at the mouth of the Mississippi.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_courses">Prehistoric courses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Prehistoric courses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The current form of the Mississippi River basin was largely shaped by the <a href="/wiki/Laurentide_Ice_Sheet" title="Laurentide Ice Sheet">Laurentide Ice Sheet</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">most recent</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_Age" title="Ice Age">Ice Age</a>. The southernmost extent of this enormous glaciation extended well into the present-day United States and Mississippi basin. When the ice sheet began to recede, hundreds of feet of rich sediment were deposited, creating the flat and fertile landscape of the Mississippi Valley. During the melt, giant glacial rivers found drainage paths into the Mississippi watershed, creating such features as the <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_River" title="Minnesota River">Minnesota River</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_River_(Dakotas)" title="James River (Dakotas)">James River</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milk_River_(Montana-Alberta)" title="Milk River (Montana-Alberta)">Milk River</a> valleys. When the ice sheet completely retreated, many of these "temporary" rivers found paths to <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a> or the Arctic Ocean, leaving the Mississippi Basin with many features "oversized" for the existing rivers to have carved in the same time period.</p>
<p>Ice sheets during the <a href="/wiki/Illinoian_(stage)" title="Illinoian (stage)">Illinoian Stage</a> about 300,000 to 132,000 years before present, blocked the Mississippi near Rock Island, Illinois, diverting it to its present channel farther to the west, the current western border of Illinois. The <a href="/wiki/Hennepin_Canal_Parkway_State_Park" title="Hennepin Canal Parkway State Park">Hennepin Canal</a> roughly follows the ancient channel of the Mississippi downstream from Rock Island to <a href="/wiki/Hennepin,_Illinois" title="Hennepin, Illinois">Hennepin, Illinois</a>. South of Hennepin, to <a href="/wiki/Alton,_Illinois" title="Alton, Illinois">Alton, Illinois</a>, the current <a href="/wiki/Illinois_River" title="Illinois River">Illinois River</a> follows the ancient channel used by the Mississippi River before the Illinoian Stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKay2007_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKay2007-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKayOther2008_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKayOther2008-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reverie_TN_08_former_MS_river_S.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Reverie_TN_08_former_MS_river_S.jpg/220px-Reverie_TN_08_former_MS_river_S.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Reverie_TN_08_former_MS_river_S.jpg/330px-Reverie_TN_08_former_MS_river_S.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Reverie_TN_08_former_MS_river_S.jpg/440px-Reverie_TN_08_former_MS_river_S.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reverie_TN_08_former_MS_river_S.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View along the former riverbed at the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>/<a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> state line near <a href="/wiki/Reverie,_Tennessee" title="Reverie, Tennessee">Reverie, Tennessee</a> (2007)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Timeline of outflow course changes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>c.5000 BC: The last <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_Age" title="Ice Age">Ice Age</a> ended; world sea level became what it is now.</li>
<li>c.2500 BC: <a href="/wiki/Bayou_Teche" title="Bayou Teche">Bayou Teche</a> became the main course of the Mississippi.</li>
<li>c.800 BC: The Mississippi diverted further east.</li>
<li>c.200 AD: <a href="/wiki/Bayou_Lafourche" title="Bayou Lafourche">Bayou Lafourche</a> became the main course of the Mississippi.</li>
<li>c.1000 AD: The Mississippi's present course took over.</li>
<li>Before c.1400 AD: The <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_South" title="Red River of the South">Red River of the South</a> flowed parallel to the lower Mississippi to the sea</li>
<li>15th century: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turnbull%27s_Bend" title="Turnbull's Bend">Turnbull's Bend</a> in the lower Mississippi extended so far west that it captured the Red River of the South. The Red River below the captured section became the <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_River" title="Atchafalaya River">Atchafalaya River</a>.</li>
<li>1831: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_Henry_M._Shreve" title="Captain Henry M. Shreve">Captain Henry M. Shreve</a> dug a new short course for the Mississippi through the neck of Turnbull's Bend.</li>
<li>1833 to November 1873: The <a href="/wiki/Great_Raft" title="Great Raft">Great Raft</a> (a huge <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logjam" title="Logjam">logjam</a> in the Atchafalaya River) was cleared. The Atchafalaya started to capture the Mississippi and to become its new main lower course.</li>
<li>1963: The <a href="/wiki/Old_River_Control_Structure" title="Old River Control Structure">Old River Control Structure</a> was completed, controlling how much Mississippi water entered the Atchafalaya.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://popular-archaeology.com/issue/spring-2015/article/cahokia-s-rise-and-fall-linked-to-river-flooding" rel="nofollow">Cahokia's rise and fall linked to river flooding</a> (article in Popular Archaeology periodical)</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Historic_course_changes">Historic course changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Historic course changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In March 1876, the Mississippi suddenly changed course near the settlement of <a href="/wiki/Reverie,_Tennessee" title="Reverie, Tennessee">Reverie, Tennessee</a>, leaving a small part of <a href="/wiki/Tipton_County,_Tennessee" title="Tipton County, Tennessee">Tipton County, Tennessee</a>, attached to <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> and separated from the rest of <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a> by the new river channel. Since this event was an <a href="/wiki/Avulsion_(river)" title="Avulsion (river)">avulsion</a>, rather than the effect of incremental erosion and deposition, the state line still follows the old channel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arkansas_v._Tennessee.2C_246_U.S._158_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arkansas_v._Tennessee.2C_246_U.S._158-37">[37]</a></sup> The town of <a href="/wiki/Kaskaskia,_Illinois" title="Kaskaskia, Illinois">Kaskaskia, Illinois</a> once stood on a peninsula at the confluence of the Mississippi and <a href="/wiki/Kaskaskia_River" title="Kaskaskia River">Kaskaskia (Okaw) Rivers</a>. Founded as a French colonial community, it later became the capital of the Illinois Territory and was the first state capital of Illinois until 1819. Beginning in 1844, successive flooding caused the Mississippi River to slowly encroach east. A major flood in 1881 caused it to overtake the lower 10 miles of the Kaskaskia River, forming a new Mississippi channel and cutting off the town from the rest of the state. Later flooding destroyed most of the remaining town, including the original State House. Today, the remaining 2,300 acre island and community of 14 residents is known as an enclave of Illinois and is accessible only from the Missouri side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Madrid_Seismic_Zone">New Madrid Seismic Zone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: New Madrid Seismic Zone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/New_Madrid_Seismic_Zone" title="New Madrid Seismic Zone">New Madrid Seismic Zone</a>, along the Mississippi River near <a href="/wiki/New_Madrid,_Missouri" title="New Madrid, Missouri">New Madrid, Missouri</a>, between Memphis and St. Louis, is related to an <a href="/wiki/Aulacogen" title="Aulacogen">aulacogen</a> (failed rift) that formed at the same time as the Gulf of Mexico. This area is still quite active seismically. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1812_New_Madrid_earthquake" title="1812 New Madrid earthquake">Four great earthquakes in 1811 and 1812</a>, estimated at approximately 8 on the <a href="/wiki/Richter_magnitude_scale" title="Richter magnitude scale">Richter magnitude scale</a>, had tremendous local effects in the then sparsely settled area, and were felt in many other places in the midwestern and eastern U.S. These earthquakes created <a href="/wiki/Reelfoot_Lake" title="Reelfoot Lake">Reelfoot Lake</a> in Tennessee from the altered landscape near the river.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_geography">Cultural geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cultural geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_boundaries">State boundaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: State boundaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Mississippi River runs through or along 10 states, from <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a> to <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, and was used to define portions of these states' borders, with <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> along the east side of the river, and <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> along its west side. Substantial parts of both Minnesota and Louisiana are on either side of the river, although the Mississippi defines part of the boundary of each of these states.</p>
<p>In all of these cases, the middle of the riverbed at the time the borders were established was used as the line to define the borders between adjacent states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In various areas, the river has since shifted, but the state borders have not changed, still following the former bed of the Mississippi River as of their establishment, leaving several small isolated areas of one state across the new river channel, contiguous with the adjacent state. Also, due to a meander in the river, a <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_Bend" title="Kentucky Bend">small part of western Kentucky</a> is contiguous with Tennessee, but isolated from the rest of its state.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bridge_crossings">Bridge crossings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Bridge crossings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_crossings_of_the_Upper_Mississippi_River" title="List of crossings of the Upper Mississippi River">List of crossings of the Upper Mississippi River</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_crossings_of_the_Lower_Mississippi_River" title="List of crossings of the Lower Mississippi River">List of crossings of the Lower Mississippi River</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_from_the_Guthrie_Theater.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Mississippi_River_from_the_Guthrie_Theater.jpg/220px-Mississippi_River_from_the_Guthrie_Theater.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Mississippi_River_from_the_Guthrie_Theater.jpg/330px-Mississippi_River_from_the_Guthrie_Theater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Mississippi_River_from_the_Guthrie_Theater.jpg/440px-Mississippi_River_from_the_Guthrie_Theater.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_from_the_Guthrie_Theater.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Stone_Arch_Bridge_(Minneapolis)" title="Stone Arch Bridge (Minneapolis)">Stone Arch Bridge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Third_Avenue_Bridge_(Minneapolis)" title="Third Avenue Bridge (Minneapolis)">Third Avenue Bridge</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hennepin_Avenue_Bridge" title="Hennepin Avenue Bridge">Hennepin Avenue Bridge</a> in Minneapolis (2004)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The road crossing highest on the Upper Mississippi is a simple steel culvert, through which the river (locally named "Nicolet Creek") flows north from Lake Nicolet under "Wilderness Road" to the West Arm of Lake Itasca, within <a href="/wiki/Itasca_State_Park" title="Itasca State Park">Itasca State Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The earliest bridge across the Mississippi River was built in 1855. It spanned the river in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minneapolis,_Minnesota" title="Minneapolis, Minnesota">Minneapolis, Minnesota</a> where the current <a href="/wiki/Hennepin_Avenue_Bridge" title="Hennepin Avenue Bridge">Hennepin Avenue Bridge</a> is located.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> No highway or railroad tunnels cross under the Mississippi River.</p>
<p>The first railroad bridge across the Mississippi was built in 1856. It spanned the river between the <a href="/wiki/Rock_Island_Arsenal" title="Rock Island Arsenal">Rock Island Arsenal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a> and Davenport, Iowa. Steamboat captains of the day, fearful of competition from the railroads, considered the new bridge a hazard to navigation. Two weeks after the bridge opened, the steamboat <i>Effie Afton</i> rammed part of the bridge, setting it on fire. Legal proceedings ensued, with <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> defending the railroad. The lawsuit went to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>, which ruled in favor of the railroad.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Below is a general overview of selected Mississippi bridges which have notable engineering or landmark significance, with their cities or locations. They are sequenced from the Upper Mississippi's source to the Lower Mississippi's mouth.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Stone_Arch_Bridge_(Minneapolis)" title="Stone Arch Bridge (Minneapolis)">Stone Arch Bridge</a> – Former <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_Railway_(U.S.)" title="Great Northern Railway (U.S.)">Great Northern Railway</a> (now pedestrian) bridge at <a href="/wiki/Saint_Anthony_Falls" title="Saint Anthony Falls">Saint Anthony Falls</a> connecting downtown Minneapolis with the historic Marcy-Holmes neighborhood.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/I-35W_Saint_Anthony_Falls_Bridge" title="I-35W Saint Anthony Falls Bridge">I-35W Saint Anthony Falls Bridge</a> – In Minneapolis, opened in September 2008, replacing the <a href="/wiki/I-35W_Mississippi_River_bridge" title="I-35W Mississippi River bridge">I-35W Mississippi River bridge</a> which had collapsed catastrophically on August 1, 2007, killing 13 and injuring over 100.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Bridge_(Mississippi_River)" title="Eisenhower Bridge (Mississippi River)">Eisenhower Bridge (Mississippi River)</a> – In <a href="/wiki/Red_Wing,_Minnesota" title="Red Wing, Minnesota">Red Wing, Minnesota</a>, opened by <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> in November 1960.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/I-90_Mississippi_River_Bridge" title="I-90 Mississippi River Bridge">I-90 Mississippi River Bridge</a> – Connects <a href="/wiki/La_Crosse,_Wisconsin" title="La Crosse, Wisconsin">La Crosse, Wisconsin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Winona_County,_Minnesota" title="Winona County, Minnesota">Winona County, Minnesota</a>, located just south of <a href="/wiki/Lock_and_Dam_No._7" title="Lock and Dam No. 7">Lock and Dam No. 7</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Black_Hawk_Bridge" title="Black Hawk Bridge">Black Hawk Bridge</a> – Connects <a href="/wiki/Lansing,_Iowa" title="Lansing, Iowa">Lansing</a> in <a href="/wiki/Allamakee_County,_Iowa" title="Allamakee County, Iowa">Allamakee County, Iowa</a> and rural <a href="/wiki/Crawford_County,_Wisconsin" title="Crawford County, Wisconsin">Crawford County, Wisconsin</a>; locally referred to as the Lansing Bridge and documented in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historic_American_Buildings_Survey" title="Historic American Buildings Survey">Historic American Engineering Record</a>.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DubWisBridge051904.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="480" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/DubWisBridge051904.jpg/220px-DubWisBridge051904.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/DubWisBridge051904.jpg/330px-DubWisBridge051904.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/DubWisBridge051904.jpg/440px-DubWisBridge051904.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DubWisBridge051904.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dubuque-Wisconsin_Bridge" title="Dubuque-Wisconsin Bridge">Dubuque-Wisconsin Bridge</a> (2004)</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dubuque-Wisconsin_Bridge" title="Dubuque-Wisconsin Bridge">Dubuque-Wisconsin Bridge</a> – Connects <a href="/wiki/Dubuque,_Iowa" title="Dubuque, Iowa">Dubuque, Iowa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grant_County,_Wisconsin" title="Grant County, Wisconsin">Grant County, Wisconsin</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Julien_Dubuque_Bridge" title="Julien Dubuque Bridge">Julien Dubuque Bridge</a> – Joins the cities of <a href="/wiki/Dubuque,_Iowa" title="Dubuque, Iowa">Dubuque, Iowa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_Dubuque,_Illinois" title="East Dubuque, Illinois">East Dubuque, Illinois</a>; listed in the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savanna-Sabula_Bridge" title="Savanna-Sabula Bridge">Savanna-Sabula Bridge</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Truss_bridge" title="Truss bridge">truss bridge</a> and causeway connecting the city of <a href="/wiki/Savanna,_Illinois" title="Savanna, Illinois">Savanna, Illinois</a>, and the island city of <a href="/wiki/Sabula,_Iowa" title="Sabula, Iowa">Sabula, Iowa</a>. The bridge carries <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Highway_52" title="U.S. Highway 52">U.S. Highway 52</a> over the river, and is the terminus of both <a href="/wiki/Iowa_Highway_64" title="Iowa Highway 64">Iowa Highway 64</a> and <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Route_64" title="Illinois Route 64">Illinois Route 64</a>. Added to the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> in 1999.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fred_Schwengel_Memorial_Bridge" title="Fred Schwengel Memorial Bridge">Fred Schwengel Memorial Bridge</a> – A 4-lane steel girder bridge that carries <a href="/wiki/Interstate_80" title="Interstate 80">Interstate 80</a> and connects <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LeClaire,_Iowa" title="LeClaire, Iowa">LeClaire, Iowa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rapids_City,_Illinois" title="Rapids City, Illinois">Rapids City, Illinois</a>. Completed in 1966.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/I-74_Bridge" title="I-74 Bridge">I-74 Bridge</a> – Connects <a href="/wiki/Bettendorf,_Iowa" title="Bettendorf, Iowa">Bettendorf, Iowa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Moline,_Illinois" title="Moline, Illinois">Moline, Illinois</a>; originally known as the <i>Iowa-Illinois Memorial Bridge</i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Government_Bridge" title="Government Bridge">Government Bridge</a> – Connects <a href="/wiki/Rock_Island,_Illinois" title="Rock Island, Illinois">Rock Island, Illinois</a> and <a href="/wiki/Davenport,_Iowa" title="Davenport, Iowa">Davenport, Iowa</a>, adjacent to <a href="/wiki/Lock_and_Dam_No._15" title="Lock and Dam No. 15">Lock and Dam No. 15</a>; the fourth crossing in this vicinity, built in 1896.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rock_Island_Centennial_Bridge" title="Rock Island Centennial Bridge">Rock Island Centennial Bridge</a> – Connects <a href="/wiki/Rock_Island,_Illinois" title="Rock Island, Illinois">Rock Island, Illinois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Davenport,_Iowa" title="Davenport, Iowa">Davenport, Iowa</a>; opened in 1940.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sergeant_John_F._Baker,_Jr._Bridge" title="Sergeant John F. Baker, Jr. Bridge">Sergeant John F. Baker, Jr. Bridge</a> – Connects <a href="/wiki/Rock_Island,_Illinois" title="Rock Island, Illinois">Rock Island, Illinois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Davenport,_Iowa" title="Davenport, Iowa">Davenport, Iowa</a>; opened in 1973.
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muscatine-ia-bridge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="253" data-file-width="360" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Muscatine-ia-bridge.jpg/220px-Muscatine-ia-bridge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Muscatine-ia-bridge.jpg/330px-Muscatine-ia-bridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1b/Muscatine-ia-bridge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muscatine-ia-bridge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Norbert F. Beckey bridge at <a href="/wiki/Muscatine,_Iowa" title="Muscatine, Iowa">Muscatine</a>, Iowa, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED" title="LED">LED</a> lighting</div>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Norbert_F._Beckey_Bridge" title="Norbert F. Beckey Bridge">Norbert F. Beckey Bridge</a> – Connects <a href="/wiki/Muscatine,_Iowa" title="Muscatine, Iowa">Muscatine, Iowa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rock_Island_County,_Illinois" title="Rock Island County, Illinois">Rock Island County, Illinois</a>; became first U.S. bridge to be illuminated with <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">light-emitting diode</a> (LED) lights decoratively illuminating the facade of the bridge.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Great_River_Bridge" title="Great River Bridge">Great River Bridge</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Cable-stayed_bridge" title="Cable-stayed bridge">cable-stayed bridge</a> connecting Burlington, Iowa, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_Port,_Illinois" title="Gulf Port, Illinois">Gulf Port, Illinois</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fort_Madison_Toll_Bridge" title="Fort Madison Toll Bridge">Fort Madison Toll Bridge</a> – Connects <a href="/wiki/Fort_Madison,_Iowa" title="Fort Madison, Iowa">Fort Madison, Iowa</a>, and unincorporated <a href="/wiki/Niota,_Illinois" title="Niota, Illinois">Niota, Illinois</a>; also known as the <i>Santa Fe Swing Span Bridge</i>; at the time of its construction the longest and heaviest electrified swing span on the Mississippi River. Listed in the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> since 1999.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Keokuk%E2%80%93Hamilton_Bridge" title="Keokuk–Hamilton Bridge">Keokuk–Hamilton Bridge</a> – Connects <a href="/wiki/Keokuk,_Iowa" title="Keokuk, Iowa">Keokuk, Iowa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Illinois" title="Hamilton, Illinois">Hamilton, Illinois</a>; opened in 1985 replacing an older bridge which is still in use as a railroad bridge.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bayview_Bridge" title="Bayview Bridge">Bayview Bridge</a> – A cable-stayed bridge bringing westbound <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Highway_24" title="U.S. Highway 24">U.S. Highway 24</a> over the river, connecting the cities of <a href="/wiki/West_Quincy,_Missouri" title="West Quincy, Missouri">West Quincy, Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quincy,_Illinois" title="Quincy, Illinois">Quincy, Illinois</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Quincy_Memorial_Bridge" title="Quincy Memorial Bridge">Quincy Memorial Bridge</a> – Connects the cities of <a href="/wiki/West_Quincy,_Missouri" title="West Quincy, Missouri">West Quincy, Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quincy,_Illinois" title="Quincy, Illinois">Quincy, Illinois</a>, carrying eastbound U.S. 24, the older of these two U.S. 24 bridges.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Clark_Bridge" title="Clark Bridge">Clark Bridge</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Cable-stayed_bridge" title="Cable-stayed bridge">cable-stayed bridge</a> connecting <a href="/wiki/West_Alton,_Missouri" title="West Alton, Missouri">West Alton, Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alton,_Illinois" title="Alton, Illinois">Alton, Illinois</a>, also known as the <i>Super Bridge</i> as the result of an appearance on the PBS program, <i><a href="/wiki/Nova_(TV_series)" title="Nova (TV series)">Nova</a></i>; built in 1994, carrying <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_67" title="U.S. Route 67">U.S. Route 67</a> across the river. This is the northernmost river crossing in the St. Louis metropolitan area, replacing the <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Clark_Bridge" title="Old Clark Bridge">Old Clark Bridge</a></i>, a truss bridge built in 1928, named after explorer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Clark_(explorer)" title="William Clark (explorer)">William Clark</a>.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChainOfRocksBridge_StLouisMO.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1389" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/ChainOfRocksBridge_StLouisMO.jpg/220px-ChainOfRocksBridge_StLouisMO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/ChainOfRocksBridge_StLouisMO.jpg/330px-ChainOfRocksBridge_StLouisMO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/ChainOfRocksBridge_StLouisMO.jpg/440px-ChainOfRocksBridge_StLouisMO.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChainOfRocksBridge_StLouisMO.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Chain_of_Rocks_Bridge" title="Chain of Rocks Bridge">Chain of Rocks Bridge</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis, Missouri</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chain_of_Rocks_Bridge" title="Chain of Rocks Bridge">Chain of Rocks Bridge</a> – Located on the northern edge of St. Louis, notable for a 22-degree bend occurring at the middle of the crossing, necessary for navigation on the river; formerly used by <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_66" title="U.S. Route 66">U.S. Route 66</a> to cross the Mississippi. Replaced for road traffic in 1966 by <a href="/wiki/New_Chain_of_Rocks_Bridge" title="New Chain of Rocks Bridge">a nearby pair of new bridges</a>; now a pedestrian bridge.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eads_Bridge" title="Eads Bridge">Eads Bridge</a> – A combined road and railway bridge, connecting St. Louis and <a href="/wiki/East_St._Louis,_Illinois" title="East St. Louis, Illinois">East St. Louis, Illinois</a>. When completed in 1874, it was the longest arch bridge in the world, with an overall length of 6,442 feet (1,964 m). The three ribbed steel arch spans were considered daring, as was the use of steel as a primary structural material; it was the first such use of true steel in a major bridge project.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chester_Bridge" title="Chester Bridge">Chester Bridge</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Truss_bridge" title="Truss bridge">truss bridge</a> connecting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Route_51_(Missouri)" title="Route 51 (Missouri)">Route 51</a> in Missouri with <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Route_150" title="Illinois Route 150">Illinois Route 150</a>, between <a href="/wiki/Perryville,_Missouri" title="Perryville, Missouri">Perryville, Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chester,_Illinois" title="Chester, Illinois">Chester, Illinois</a>. The bridge can be seen in the beginning of the 1967 film <i><a href="/wiki/In_the_Heat_of_the_Night_(film)" title="In the Heat of the Night (film)">In the Heat of the Night</a></i>. In the 1940s, the main span was destroyed by a <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornado</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bill_Emerson_Memorial_Bridge" title="Bill Emerson Memorial Bridge">Bill Emerson Memorial Bridge</a>—Connecting <a href="/wiki/Cape_Girardeau,_Missouri" title="Cape Girardeau, Missouri">Cape Girardeau, Missouri</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Cape_Girardeau,_Illinois" title="East Cape Girardeau, Illinois">East Cape Girardeau, Illinois</a>, completed in 2003 and illuminated by 140 lights.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Caruthersville_Bridge" title="Caruthersville Bridge">Caruthersville Bridge</a> –  A single tower cantilever bridge carrying <a href="/wiki/Interstate_155_(Missouri%E2%80%93Tennessee)" title="Interstate 155 (Missouri–Tennessee)">Interstate 155</a> and <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_412" title="U.S. Route 412">U.S. Route 412</a> across the Mississippi River between <a href="/wiki/Caruthersville,_Missouri" title="Caruthersville, Missouri">Caruthersville, Missouri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dyersburg,_Tennessee" title="Dyersburg, Tennessee">Dyersburg, Tennessee</a>.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Memphis_Bridge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3456" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Memphis_Bridge.jpg/220px-Memphis_Bridge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Memphis_Bridge.jpg/330px-Memphis_Bridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Memphis_Bridge.jpg/440px-Memphis_Bridge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Memphis_Bridge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Hernando_de_Soto_Bridge" title="Hernando de Soto Bridge">Hernando de Soto Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis, Tennessee</a> (2009)</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hernando_de_Soto_Bridge" title="Hernando de Soto Bridge">Hernando de Soto Bridge</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Through_arch_bridge" title="Through arch bridge">through arch bridge</a> carrying <a href="/wiki/Interstate_40" title="Interstate 40">Interstate 40</a> across the Mississippi between <a href="/wiki/West_Memphis,_Arkansas" title="West Memphis, Arkansas">West Memphis, Arkansas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis, Tennessee</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harahan_Bridge" title="Harahan Bridge">Harahan Bridge</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Cantilever_bridge" title="Cantilever bridge">cantilevered</a> <a href="/wiki/Truss_bridge" title="Truss bridge">through truss bridge</a>, carrying two rail lines of the <a href="/wiki/Union_Pacific_Railroad" title="Union Pacific Railroad">Union Pacific Railroad</a> across the river between <a href="/wiki/West_Memphis,_Arkansas" title="West Memphis, Arkansas">West Memphis, Arkansas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis, Tennessee</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frisco_Bridge" title="Frisco Bridge">Frisco Bridge</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Cantilever_bridge" title="Cantilever bridge">cantilevered</a> <a href="/wiki/Truss_bridge" title="Truss bridge">through truss bridge</a>, carrying a rail line across the river between <a href="/wiki/West_Memphis,_Arkansas" title="West Memphis, Arkansas">West Memphis, Arkansas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis, Tennessee</a>, previously known as the <i>Memphis Bridge</i>. When it opened on May 12, 1892, it was the first crossing of the Lower Mississippi and the longest span in the U.S. Listed as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_historic_civil_engineering_landmarks" title="List of historic civil engineering landmarks">Historic Civil Engineering Landmark</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Memphis_%26_Arkansas_Bridge" title="Memphis &amp; Arkansas Bridge">Memphis &amp; Arkansas Bridge</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Cantilever_bridge" title="Cantilever bridge">cantilevered</a> <a href="/wiki/Truss_bridge" title="Truss bridge">through truss bridge</a>, carrying <a href="/wiki/Interstate_55" title="Interstate 55">Interstate 55</a> between Memphis and West Memphis; listed on the National Register of Historic Places.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Helena_Bridge" title="Helena Bridge">Helena Bridge</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greenville_Bridge" title="Greenville Bridge">Greenville Bridge</a></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:16_10_033_vicksburg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1383" data-file-width="2048" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/16_10_033_vicksburg.jpg/220px-16_10_033_vicksburg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/16_10_033_vicksburg.jpg/330px-16_10_033_vicksburg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/16_10_033_vicksburg.jpg/440px-16_10_033_vicksburg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Old_Vicksburg_Bridge" title="Old Vicksburg Bridge">Old Vicksburg Bridge</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_Bridge" title="Vicksburg Bridge">Vicksburg Bridge</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natchez-Vidalia_Bridge" title="Natchez-Vidalia Bridge">Natchez-Vidalia Bridge</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_James_Audubon_Bridge_(Mississippi_River)" title="John James Audubon Bridge (Mississippi River)">John James Audubon Bridge</a> – The second-longest <a href="/wiki/Cable-stayed_bridge" title="Cable-stayed bridge">cable-stayed bridge</a> in the Western Hemisphere; connects <a href="/wiki/Pointe_Coupee_Parish,_Louisiana" title="Pointe Coupee Parish, Louisiana">Pointe Coupee</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Feliciana_Parish,_Louisiana" title="West Feliciana Parish, Louisiana">West Feliciana</a> Parishes in Louisiana. It is the only crossing between Baton Rouge and Natchez. This bridge was opened a month ahead of schedule in May 2011, due to the <a href="/wiki/2011_Mississippi_River_floods" title="2011 Mississippi River floods">2011 floods</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Huey_P._Long_Bridge_(Baton_Rouge)" title="Huey P. Long Bridge (Baton Rouge)">Huey P. Long Bridge</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Truss" title="Truss">truss</a> <a href="/wiki/Cantilever_bridge" title="Cantilever bridge">cantilever bridge</a> carrying <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_190" title="U.S. Route 190">US 190</a> (<a href="/wiki/Airline_Highway" title="Airline Highway">Airline Highway</a>) and one rail line between <a href="/wiki/East_Baton_Rouge_Parish,_Louisiana" title="East Baton Rouge Parish, Louisiana">East Baton Rouge</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Baton_Rouge_Parish,_Louisiana" title="West Baton Rouge Parish, Louisiana">West Baton Rouge</a> Parishes in Louisiana.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Horace_Wilkinson_Bridge" title="Horace Wilkinson Bridge">Horace Wilkinson Bridge</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Cantilever_bridge" title="Cantilever bridge">cantilevered</a> <a href="/wiki/Truss_bridge" title="Truss bridge">through truss</a> bridge, carrying six lanes of <a href="/wiki/Interstate_10" title="Interstate 10">Interstate 10</a> between <a href="/wiki/Baton_Rouge,_Louisiana" title="Baton Rouge, Louisiana">Baton Rouge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Port_Allen,_Louisiana" title="Port Allen, Louisiana">Port Allen</a> in Louisiana. It is the highest bridge over the Mississippi River.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sunshine_Bridge" title="Sunshine Bridge">Sunshine Bridge</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gramercy_Bridge" title="Gramercy Bridge">Gramercy Bridge</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Huey_P._Long_Bridge_(Jefferson_Parish)" title="Huey P. Long Bridge (Jefferson Parish)">Huey P. Long Bridge</a> – In <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Parish,_Louisiana" title="Jefferson Parish, Louisiana">Jefferson Parish, Louisiana</a>, the first Mississippi River span built in Louisiana.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Crescent_City_Connection" title="Crescent City Connection">Crescent City Connection</a> – Connects the east and west banks of <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, Louisiana; the fifth-longest <a href="/wiki/Cantilever_bridge" title="Cantilever bridge">cantilever bridge</a> in the world.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Navigation_and_flood_control">Navigation and flood control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Navigation and flood control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_locks_and_dams_of_the_Upper_Mississippi_River" title="List of locks and dams of the Upper Mississippi River">List of locks and dams of the Upper Mississippi River</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20040711181620_Mississippi_Memphis_Ausschnitt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="782" data-file-width="1894" height="91" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/20040711181620_Mississippi_Memphis_Ausschnitt.jpg/220px-20040711181620_Mississippi_Memphis_Ausschnitt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/20040711181620_Mississippi_Memphis_Ausschnitt.jpg/330px-20040711181620_Mississippi_Memphis_Ausschnitt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/20040711181620_Mississippi_Memphis_Ausschnitt.jpg/440px-20040711181620_Mississippi_Memphis_Ausschnitt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20040711181620_Mississippi_Memphis_Ausschnitt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Towboat and barges at <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis, Tennessee</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
