<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_ship_navigation.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="893" data-file-width="1797" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Mississippi_ship_navigation.png/220px-Mississippi_ship_navigation.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Mississippi_ship_navigation.png/330px-Mississippi_ship_navigation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Mississippi_ship_navigation.png/440px-Mississippi_ship_navigation.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_ship_navigation.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ships on the lower part of the Mississippi</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A clear channel is needed for the <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">barges</a> and other vessels that make the <a href="/wiki/Main_stem" title="Main stem">main stem</a> Mississippi one of the great commercial <a href="/wiki/Waterway" title="Waterway">waterways</a> of the world. The task of maintaining a navigation channel is the responsibility of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="United States Army Corps of Engineers">United States Army Corps of Engineers</a>, which was established in 1802.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Earlier projects began as early as 1829 to remove snags, close off secondary channels and excavate rocks and <a href="/wiki/Shoal" title="Shoal">sandbars</a>.</p>
<p>Steamboats entered trade in the 1820s, so the period 1830–1850 became the golden age of steamboats. As there were few roads or rails in the lands of the Louisiana Purchase, river traffic was an ideal solution. Cotton, timber and food came down the river, as did Appalachian coal. The port of New Orleans boomed as it was the trans-shipment point to deep sea ocean vessels. As a result, the image of the twin stacked, wedding cake Mississippi steamer entered into American mythology. Steamers worked the entire route from the trickles of Montana, to the Ohio River; down the Missouri and Tennessee, to the main channel of the Mississippi. Only with the arrival of the railroads in the 1880s did steamboat traffic diminish. Steamboats remained a feature until the 1920s. Most have been superseded by pusher tugs. A few survive as icons—the <a href="/wiki/Delta_Queen" title="Delta Queen">Delta Queen</a> and the <a href="/wiki/River_Queen_(steamboat)" title="River Queen (steamboat)">River Queen</a> for instance.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ship_on_lower_Mississippi.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1504" data-file-width="3600" height="92" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ship_on_lower_Mississippi.jpeg/220px-Ship_on_lower_Mississippi.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ship_on_lower_Mississippi.jpeg/330px-Ship_on_lower_Mississippi.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ship_on_lower_Mississippi.jpeg/440px-Ship_on_lower_Mississippi.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ship_on_lower_Mississippi.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Oil tanker on the Lower Mississippi near the Port of New Orleans</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lower_Mississippi_River_barge.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2100" data-file-width="4912" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Lower_Mississippi_River_barge.png/220px-Lower_Mississippi_River_barge.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Lower_Mississippi_River_barge.png/330px-Lower_Mississippi_River_barge.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Lower_Mississippi_River_barge.png/440px-Lower_Mississippi_River_barge.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lower_Mississippi_River_barge.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Barge on the Lower Mississippi River</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A series of 29 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lock_(water_transport)" title="Lock (water transport)">locks</a> and dams on the upper Mississippi, most of which were built in the 1930s, is designed primarily to maintain a 9-foot-deep (2.7 m) channel for commercial barge traffic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The lakes formed are also used for recreational boating and fishing. The dams make the river deeper and wider but do not stop it. No flood control is intended. During periods of high flow, the gates, some of which are submersible, are completely opened and the dams simply cease to function. Below St. Louis, the Mississippi is relatively free-flowing, although it is constrained by numerous levees and directed by numerous <a href="/wiki/Wing_dam" title="Wing dam">wing dams</a>.</p>
<p>On the lower Mississippi, from <a href="/wiki/Baton_Rouge,_Louisiana" title="Baton Rouge, Louisiana">Baton Rouge</a> to the mouth of the Mississippi, the navigation depth is 45 feet (14 m), allowing container ships and cruise ships to dock at the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_New_Orleans" title="Port of New Orleans">Port of New Orleans</a> and bulk cargo ships shorter than 150-foot (46 m) air draft that fit under the <a href="/wiki/Huey_P._Long_Bridge_(Jefferson_Parish)" title="Huey P. Long Bridge (Jefferson Parish)">Huey P. Long Bridge</a> to traverse the Mississippi to Baton Rouge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> There is a <a href="/wiki/Feasibility_study" title="Feasibility study">feasibility study</a> to dredge this portion of the river to 50 feet (15 m) to allow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Panamax" title="New Panamax">New Panamax</a> ship depths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lock_and_Dam_11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Lock_and_Dam_11.jpg/220px-Lock_and_Dam_11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Lock_and_Dam_11.jpg/330px-Lock_and_Dam_11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Lock_and_Dam_11.jpg/440px-Lock_and_Dam_11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lock_and_Dam_11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lock_and_Dam_No._11" title="Lock and Dam No. 11">Lock and Dam No. 11</a>, north of Dubuque, Iowa (2007)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1829, there were surveys of the two major obstacles on the upper Mississippi, the <a href="/wiki/Des_Moines_Rapids" title="Des Moines Rapids">Des Moines Rapids</a> and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_Island_Rapids&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rock Island Rapids (page does not exist)">Rock Island Rapids</a>, where the river was shallow and the riverbed was rock. The Des Moines Rapids were about 11 miles (18 km) long and just above the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Des_Moines_River" title="Des Moines River">Des Moines River</a> at Keokuk, Iowa. The Rock Island Rapids were between <a href="/wiki/Rock_Island,_Illinois" title="Rock Island, Illinois">Rock Island</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moline,_Illinois" title="Moline, Illinois">Moline, Illinois</a>. Both rapids were considered virtually impassable.</p>
<p>In 1848, the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_and_Michigan_Canal" title="Illinois and Michigan Canal">Illinois and Michigan Canal</a> was built to connect the Mississippi River to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Lake Michigan</a> via the Illinois River near <a href="/wiki/Peru,_Illinois" title="Peru, Illinois">Peru, Illinois</a>. The canal allowed shipping between these important waterways. In 1900, the canal was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Sanitary_and_Ship_Canal" title="Chicago Sanitary and Ship Canal">Chicago Sanitary and Ship Canal</a>. The second canal, in addition to shipping, also allowed Chicago to address specific health issues (<a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> and other waterborne diseases) by sending its waste down the Illinois and Mississippi river systems rather than polluting its water source of Lake Michigan.</p>
<p>The Corps of Engineers recommended the excavation of a 5-foot-deep (1.5 m) channel at the Des Moines Rapids, but work did not begin until after Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a> endorsed the project in 1837. The Corps later also began excavating the Rock Island Rapids. By 1866, it had become evident that excavation was impractical, and it was decided to build a canal around the Des Moines Rapids. The canal opened in 1877, but the Rock Island Rapids remained an obstacle. In 1878, Congress authorized the Corps to establish a 4.5-foot-deep (1.4 m) channel to be obtained by building wing dams which direct the river to a narrow channel causing it to cut a deeper channel, by closing secondary channels and by dredging. The channel project was complete when the Moline Lock, which bypassed the Rock Island Rapids, opened in 1907.</p>
<p>To improve navigation between St. Paul, Minnesota, and <a href="/wiki/Prairie_du_Chien,_Wisconsin" title="Prairie du Chien, Wisconsin">Prairie du Chien, Wisconsin</a>, the Corps constructed several dams on lakes in the headwaters area, including <a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnibigoshish" title="Lake Winnibigoshish">Lake Winnibigoshish</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pokegama_Dam" title="Pokegama Dam">Lake Pokegama</a>. The dams, which were built beginning in the 1880s, stored spring run-off which was released during low water to help maintain channel depth.</p>
<div style="float:right;clear:right">
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1332" data-file-width="2036" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_2.jpg/220px-Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_2.jpg/330px-Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_2.jpg/440px-Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lock_and_Dam_No._2" title="Lock and Dam No. 2">Lock and Dam No. 2</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Hastings,_Minnesota" title="Hastings, Minnesota">Hastings, Minnesota</a> (2007)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_15.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1500" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_15.jpg/220px-Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_15.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_15.jpg/330px-Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_15.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_15.jpg/440px-Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_15.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_Lock_and_Dam_number_15.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lock_and_Dam_No._15" title="Lock and Dam No. 15">Lock and Dam No. 15</a>, is the largest roller dam in the world <a href="/wiki/Davenport,_Iowa" title="Davenport, Iowa">Davenport, Iowa</a>; <a href="/wiki/Rock_Island,_Illinois" title="Rock Island, Illinois">Rock Island, Illinois</a>. (1990)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1907, Congress authorized a 6-foot-deep (1.8 m) channel project on the Mississippi, which was not complete when it was abandoned in the late 1920s in favor of the 9-foot-deep (2.7 m) channel project.</p>
<p>In 1913, construction was complete on <a href="/wiki/Lock_and_Dam_No._19" title="Lock and Dam No. 19">Lock and Dam No. 19</a> at <a href="/wiki/Keokuk,_Iowa" title="Keokuk, Iowa">Keokuk, Iowa</a>, the first dam below St. Anthony Falls. Built by a private power company (<a href="/wiki/Union_Electric_Company" title="Union Electric Company">Union Electric Company</a> of St. Louis) to generate electricity (originally for <a href="/wiki/Streetcars_in_St._Louis" title="Streetcars in St. Louis">streetcars in St. Louis</a>), the Keokuk dam was one of the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydro-electric" title="Hydro-electric">hydro-electric</a> plants in the world at the time. The dam also eliminated the Des Moines Rapids. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lock_and_Dam_No._1,_Mississippi_River" title="Lock and Dam No. 1, Mississippi River">Lock and Dam No. 1</a> was completed in Minneapolis, Minnesota in 1917. <a href="/wiki/Lock_and_Dam_No._2" title="Lock and Dam No. 2">Lock and Dam No. 2</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Hastings,_Minnesota" title="Hastings, Minnesota">Hastings, Minnesota</a>, was completed in 1930.</p>
<p>Before the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_Flood_of_1927" title="Great Mississippi Flood of 1927">Great Mississippi Flood of 1927</a>, the Corps's primary strategy was to close off as many side channels as possible to increase the flow in the main river. It was thought that the river's <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> would scour off bottom <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a>, deepening the river and decreasing the possibility of flooding. The 1927 flood proved this to be so wrong that communities threatened by the flood began to create their own levee breaks to relieve the force of the rising river.</p>
<p>The Rivers and Harbors Act of 1930 authorized the 9-foot (2.7 m) channel project, which called for a navigation channel 9 feet (2.7 m) feet deep and 400 feet (120 m) wide to accommodate multiple-barge tows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> This was achieved by a series of locks and dams, and by dredging. Twenty-three new locks and dams were built on the upper Mississippi in the 1930s in addition to the three already in existence.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geomorphology_of_Old_River.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="847" data-file-width="959" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Geomorphology_of_Old_River.jpg/220px-Geomorphology_of_Old_River.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Geomorphology_of_Old_River.jpg/330px-Geomorphology_of_Old_River.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Geomorphology_of_Old_River.jpg/440px-Geomorphology_of_Old_River.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geomorphology_of_Old_River.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Formation of the Atchafalaya River and construction of the Old River Control Structure.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_flow.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="774" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Mississippi_River_flow.gif/170px-Mississippi_River_flow.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Mississippi_River_flow.gif/255px-Mississippi_River_flow.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Mississippi_River_flow.gif/340px-Mississippi_River_flow.gif 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_flow.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Project_design_flood" title="Project design flood">Project design flood</a> flow capacity for the Mississippi river in thousands of cubic feet per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USACE_2008_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-USACE_2008-50">[50]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Until the 1950s, there was no dam below Lock and Dam 26 at Alton, Illinois. <a href="/wiki/Chain_of_Rocks_Lock" title="Chain of Rocks Lock">Chain of Rocks Lock</a> (Lock and Dam No. 27), which consists of a low-water dam and an 8.4-mile-long (13.5 km) canal, was added in 1953, just below the confluence with the Missouri River, primarily to bypass a series of rock ledges at St. Louis. It also serves to protect the St. Louis city water intakes during times of low water.</p>
<p>U.S. government scientists determined in the 1950s that the Mississippi River was starting to switch to the Atchafalaya River channel because of its much steeper path to the Gulf of Mexico. Eventually the Atchafalaya River would capture the Mississippi River and become its main channel to the Gulf of Mexico, leaving New Orleans on a side channel. As a result, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">U.S. Congress</a> authorized a project called the <a href="/wiki/Old_River_Control_Structure" title="Old River Control Structure">Old River Control Structure</a>, which has prevented the Mississippi River from leaving its current channel that drains into the Gulf via New Orleans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SJSU_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-SJSU-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because the large scale of high-energy water flow threatened to damage the structure, an auxiliary flow control station was built adjacent to the standing control station. This $300 million project was completed in 1986 by the Corps of Engineers. Beginning in the 1970s, the Corps applied <a href="/wiki/Hydrological_transport_model" title="Hydrological transport model">hydrological transport models</a> to analyze flood flow and water quality of the Mississippi. Dam 26 at Alton, Illinois, which had structural problems, was replaced by the Mel Price Lock and Dam in 1990. The original Lock and Dam 26 was demolished.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dam_-27.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="683" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Dam_-27.JPG/170px-Dam_-27.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Dam_-27.JPG/255px-Dam_-27.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Dam_-27.JPG/340px-Dam_-27.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dam_-27.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A low-water dam deepens the pool above the <a href="/wiki/Chain_of_Rocks_Lock" title="Chain of Rocks Lock">Chain of Rocks Lock</a> near St. Louis (2006)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Army_mil-2008-07-17-085659.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="3000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Army_mil-2008-07-17-085659.jpg/220px-Army_mil-2008-07-17-085659.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Army_mil-2008-07-17-085659.jpg/330px-Army_mil-2008-07-17-085659.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Army_mil-2008-07-17-085659.jpg/440px-Army_mil-2008-07-17-085659.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Army_mil-2008-07-17-085659.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Soldiers of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missouri_Army_National_Guard" title="Missouri Army National Guard">Missouri Army National Guard</a> sandbag the River in <a href="/wiki/Clarksville,_Missouri" title="Clarksville, Missouri">Clarksville, Missouri</a>, June 2008, following flooding.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Corps now actively creates and maintains spillways and floodways to divert periodic water surges into backwater channels and lakes, as well as route part of the Mississippi's flow into the <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_Basin" title="Atchafalaya Basin">Atchafalaya Basin</a> and from there to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, bypassing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baton_Rouge" title="Baton Rouge">Baton Rouge</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>. The main structures are the <a href="/wiki/Birds_Point-New_Madrid_Floodway" title="Birds Point-New Madrid Floodway">Birds Point-New Madrid Floodway</a> in Missouri; the <a href="/wiki/Old_River_Control_Structure" title="Old River Control Structure">Old River Control Structure</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Morganza_Spillway" title="Morganza Spillway">Morganza Spillway</a> in Louisiana, which direct excess water down the west and east sides (respectively) of the <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_River" title="Atchafalaya River">Atchafalaya River</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Bonnet_Carr%C3%A9_Spillway" title="Bonnet Carré Spillway">Bonnet Carré Spillway</a>, also in Louisiana, which directs floodwaters to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pontchartrain" title="Lake Pontchartrain">Lake Pontchartrain</a> (see diagram). Some experts blame <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">urban sprawl</a> for increases in both the risk and frequency of flooding on the Mississippi River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Associated_Press_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Associated_Press-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the pre-1927 strategy is still in use today, with the Corps actively cutting the necks of <a href="/wiki/Meander" title="Meander">horseshoe bends</a>, allowing the water to move faster and reducing flood heights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US_Army_Corps_of_Engineers_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-US_Army_Corps_of_Engineers-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Native_Americans">Native Americans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Native Americans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Woodland_period" title="Woodland period">Woodland period</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hopewell_tradition" title="Hopewell tradition">Hopewell tradition</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mississippian_culture" title="Mississippian culture">Mississippian culture</a></div>
<div style="float:right;clear:right;">
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monks_Mound_in_July.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Monks_Mound_in_July.JPG/220px-Monks_Mound_in_July.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Monks_Mound_in_July.JPG/330px-Monks_Mound_in_July.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Monks_Mound_in_July.JPG/440px-Monks_Mound_in_July.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monks_Mound_in_July.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Monks_Mound" title="Monks Mound">Monks Mound</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Platform_mound" title="Platform mound">platform mound</a> at the site of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippian_culture" title="Mississippian culture">Mississippian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Cahokia" title="Cahokia">Cahokia</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The area of the Mississippi River basin was first <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">settled</a> by <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunting and gathering</a> <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American peoples</a> and is considered one of the few independent centers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestication_of_plants" title="Domestication of plants">plant domestication</a> in human history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Evidence of early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_cultivation" title="Plant cultivation">cultivation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Helianthus_annuus" title="Helianthus annuus">sunflower</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Chenopodium_berlandieri" title="Chenopodium berlandieri">goosefoot</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Iva_annua" title="Iva annua">marsh elder</a> and an indigenous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(plant)" title="Squash (plant)">squash</a> dates to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/4th_millennium_BCE" title="4th millennium BCE">4th millennium BCE</a>. The lifestyle gradually became more settled after around 1000 BCE during what is now called the <a href="/wiki/Woodland_period" title="Woodland period">Woodland period</a>, with increasing evidence of shelter construction, <a href="/wiki/Ceramics_of_indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Ceramics of indigenous peoples of the Americas">pottery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weaving</a> and other practices. A network of trade routes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Hopewell_tradition" title="Hopewell tradition">Hopewell interaction sphere</a> was active along the waterways between about 200 and 500 CE, spreading common cultural practices over the entire area between the Gulf of Mexico and the Great Lakes. A period of more isolated communities followed, and agriculture introduced from <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a> based on the <a href="/wiki/Three_Sisters_(agriculture)" title="Three Sisters (agriculture)">Three Sisters</a> (maize, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beans" title="Beans">beans</a> and squash) gradually came to dominate. After around 800 CE there arose an advanced agricultural society today referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Mississippian_culture" title="Mississippian culture">Mississippian culture</a>, with evidence of highly <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">stratified</a> <a href="/wiki/Complex_society" title="Complex society">complex</a> <a href="/wiki/Chiefdom" title="Chiefdom">chiefdoms</a> and large <a href="/wiki/Human_settlement" title="Human settlement">population centers</a>. The most prominent of these, now called <a href="/wiki/Cahokia" title="Cahokia">Cahokia</a>, was occupied between about 600 and 1400 CE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and at its peak numbered between 8,000 and 40,000 inhabitants, larger than London, England of that time. At the time of first contact with Europeans, Cahokia and many other Mississippian cities had dispersed, and <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a> finds attest to increased social stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Modern American Indian nations inhabiting the Mississippi basin include <a href="/wiki/Cheyenne" title="Cheyenne">Cheyenne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sioux" title="Sioux">Sioux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe" title="Ojibwe">Ojibwe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potawatomi" title="Potawatomi">Potawatomi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ho-Chunk" title="Ho-Chunk">Ho-Chunk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fox_(tribe)" title="Fox (tribe)">Fox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kickapoo_people" title="Kickapoo people">Kickapoo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamaroa_(tribe)" title="Tamaroa (tribe)">Tamaroa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moingwena" title="Moingwena">Moingwena</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quapaw" title="Quapaw">Quapaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw" title="Chickasaw">Chickasaw</a>.</p>
<p>The word <i>Mississippi</i> itself comes from <i>Messipi</i>, the French rendering of the <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe_language" title="Ojibwe language">Anishinaabe</a> (Ojibwe or Algonquin) name for the river, <i>Misi-ziibi</i> (Great River).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe" title="Ojibwe">Ojibwe</a> called Lake Itasca <i>Omashkoozo-zaaga'igan</i> (Elk Lake) and the river flowing out of it <i>Omashkoozo-ziibi</i> (Elk River). After flowing into <a href="/wiki/Lake_Bemidji" title="Lake Bemidji">Lake Bemidji</a>, the Ojibwe called the river <i>Bemijigamaag-ziibi</i> (River from the Traversing Lake). After flowing into <a href="/wiki/Cass_Lake_(Minnesota)" title="Cass Lake (Minnesota)">Cass Lake</a>, the name of the river changes to <i>Gaa-miskwaawaakokaag-ziibi</i> (Red Cedar River) and then out of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnibigoshish" title="Lake Winnibigoshish">Lake Winnibigoshish</a> as <i>Wiinibiigoonzhish-ziibi</i> (Miserable Wretched Dirty Water River), <i>Gichi-ziibi</i> (Big River) after the confluence with the <a href="/wiki/Leech_Lake_River" title="Leech Lake River">Leech Lake River</a>, then finally as <i>Misi-ziibi</i> (Great River) after the confluence with the <a href="/wiki/Crow_Wing_River" title="Crow Wing River">Crow Wing River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> After the expeditions by <a href="/wiki/Giacomo_Beltrami" title="Giacomo Beltrami">Giacomo Beltrami</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Schoolcraft" title="Henry Schoolcraft">Henry Schoolcraft</a>, the longest stream above the juncture of the Crow Wing River and <i>Gichi-ziibi</i> was named "Mississippi River". The <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River_Band_of_Chippewa_Indians" title="Mississippi River Band of Chippewa Indians">Mississippi River Band of Chippewa Indians</a>, known as the <i>Gichi-ziibiwininiwag</i>, are named after the stretch of the Mississippi River known as the <i>Gichi-ziibi</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Cheyenne" title="Cheyenne">Cheyenne</a>, one of the earliest inhabitants of the upper Mississippi River, called it the <i>Máʼxe-éʼometaaʼe</i> (Big Greasy River) in the <a href="/wiki/Cheyenne_language" title="Cheyenne language">Cheyenne language</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Arapaho_language" title="Arapaho language">Arapaho</a> name for the river is <i>Beesniicíe</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pawnee_language" title="Pawnee language">Pawnee</a> name is <i>Kickaátit</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Mississippi was spelled Mississipi or Missisipi during French Louisiana and was also known as the Rivière Saint-Louis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_exploration">European exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: European exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Discovery_of_the_Mississippi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1948" data-file-width="3000" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Discovery_of_the_Mississippi.jpg/220px-Discovery_of_the_Mississippi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Discovery_of_the_Mississippi.jpg/330px-Discovery_of_the_Mississippi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Discovery_of_the_Mississippi.jpg/440px-Discovery_of_the_Mississippi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Discovery_of_the_Mississippi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Discovery of the Mississippi by De Soto A.D. 1541</i> by <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Powell" title="William Henry Powell">William Henry Powell</a> depicts <a href="/wiki/Hernando_de_Soto" title="Hernando de Soto">Hernando De Soto</a> and Spanish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conquistadores" title="Conquistadores">Conquistadores</a> seeing the Mississippi River for the first time.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marquette_and_jolliet_map_1681.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2063" data-file-width="860" height="408" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Marquette_and_jolliet_map_1681.jpg/170px-Marquette_and_jolliet_map_1681.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Marquette_and_jolliet_map_1681.jpg/255px-Marquette_and_jolliet_map_1681.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Marquette_and_jolliet_map_1681.jpg/340px-Marquette_and_jolliet_map_1681.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marquette_and_jolliet_map_1681.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ca. 1681 map of <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Marquette" title="Jacques Marquette">Marquette</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jolliet" title="Louis Jolliet">Jolliet</a>'s 1673 expedition.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On May 8, 1541, Spanish explorer <a href="/wiki/Hernando_de_Soto" title="Hernando de Soto">Hernando de Soto</a> became the first recorded European to reach the Mississippi River, which he called <i>Río del Espíritu Santo</i> ("River of the Holy Spirit"), in the area of what is now Mississippi. In Spanish, the river is called <i>Río Mississippi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>French explorers <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jolliet" title="Louis Jolliet">Louis Jolliet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Marquette" title="Jacques Marquette">Jacques Marquette</a> began exploring the Mississippi in the 17th century. Marquette traveled with a <a href="/wiki/Sioux" title="Sioux">Sioux</a> Indian who named it <i>Ne Tongo</i> ("Big river" in <a href="/wiki/Sioux_language" title="Sioux language">Sioux language</a>) in 1673. Marquette proposed calling it the <i>River of the <a href="/wiki/Immaculate_Conception" title="Immaculate Conception">Immaculate Conception</a></i>.</p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jolliet" title="Louis Jolliet">Louis Jolliet</a> explored the Mississippi Valley in the 17th century, natives guided him to a quicker way to return to French Canada via the Illinois River. When he found the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Portage" title="Chicago Portage">Chicago Portage</a>, he remarked that a canal of "only half a <a href="/wiki/League_(unit)" title="League (unit)">league</a>" (less than 2 miles (3.2 km), 3 km) would join the Mississippi and the Great Lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In 1848, the <a href="/wiki/Continental_divide" title="Continental divide">continental divide</a> separating the waters of the Great Lakes and the Mississippi Valley was breached by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illinois_and_Michigan_canal" title="Illinois and Michigan canal">Illinois and Michigan canal</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_River" title="Chicago River">Chicago River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> This both accelerated the development, and forever changed the ecology of the Mississippi Valley and the Great Lakes.</p>
<p>In 1682, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9-Robert_Cavelier,_Sieur_de_La_Salle" title="René-Robert Cavelier, Sieur de La Salle">René-Robert Cavelier, Sieur de La Salle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henri_de_Tonti" title="Henri de Tonti">Henri de Tonti</a> claimed the entire Mississippi River Valley for France, calling the river <i>Colbert River</i> after <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Colbert" title="Jean-Baptiste Colbert">Jean-Baptiste Colbert</a> and the region <i><a href="/wiki/Louisiana_(New_France)" title="Louisiana (New France)">La Louisiane</a></i>, for <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">King Louis XIV</a>. On March 2, 1699, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Le_Moyne_d%27Iberville" title="Pierre Le Moyne d'Iberville">Pierre Le Moyne d'Iberville</a> rediscovered the mouth of the Mississippi, following the death of La Salle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CathEn_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-CathEn-70">[70]</a></sup> The French built the small fort of <a href="/wiki/La_Balize,_Louisiana" title="La Balize, Louisiana">La Balise</a> there to control passage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WDL1_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-WDL1-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1718, about 100 miles (160 km) upriver, New Orleans was established along the river crescent by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Le_Moyne,_Sieur_de_Bienville" title="Jean-Baptiste Le Moyne, Sieur de Bienville">Jean-Baptiste Le Moyne, Sieur de Bienville</a>, with construction patterned after the 1711 resettlement on Mobile Bay of <a href="/wiki/Mobile,_Alabama" title="Mobile, Alabama">Mobile</a>, the capital of French Louisiana at the time.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonization">Colonization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Colonization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Flood_of_1851" title="Flood of 1851">Flood of 1851</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Home_on_the_Mississippi.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1264" data-file-width="2014" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/A_Home_on_the_Mississippi.png/220px-A_Home_on_the_Mississippi.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/A_Home_on_the_Mississippi.png/330px-A_Home_on_the_Mississippi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/A_Home_on_the_Mississippi.png/440px-A_Home_on_the_Mississippi.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Home_on_the_Mississippi.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/A_Home_on_the_Mississippi" title="A Home on the Mississippi">A Home on the Mississippi</a></i> (1871)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Britain_in_the_Seven_Years_War" title="Great Britain in the Seven Years War">Britain's victory in the Seven Years War</a> the Mississippi became the border between the British and Spanish Empires. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1763)" title="Treaty of Paris (1763)">Treaty of Paris (1763)</a> gave <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a> rights to all land east of the Mississippi and Spain rights to land west of the Mississippi. Spain also ceded <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Florida" title="Spanish Florida">Florida</a> to Britain to regain <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, which the British occupied during the war. Britain then divided the territory into <a href="/wiki/East_Florida" title="East Florida">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Florida" title="West Florida">West Florida</a>.</p>
<p>Article 8 of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris (1783)</a> states, "The navigation of the river Mississippi, from its source to the ocean, shall forever remain free and open to the subjects of Great Britain and the citizens of the United States". With this treaty, which ended the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, Britain also ceded West Florida back to Spain to regain <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">the Bahamas</a>, which Spain had occupied during the war. In 1800, under duress from Napoleon of France, Spain ceded an undefined portion of West Florida to France. When France then sold the Louisiana Territory to the U.S. in 1803, a dispute arose again between Spain and the U.S. on which parts of West Florida exactly had Spain ceded to France, which would in turn decide which parts of West Florida were now U.S. property versus Spanish property. These aspirations ended when Spain was pressured into signing <a href="/wiki/Pinckney%27s_Treaty" title="Pinckney's Treaty">Pinckney's Treaty</a> in 1795.</p>
<p>France reacquired 'Louisiana' from Spain in the secret <a href="/wiki/Third_Treaty_of_San_Ildefonso" title="Third Treaty of San Ildefonso">Treaty of San Ildefonso</a> in 1800. The United States then secured effective control of the river when it bought the Louisiana Territory from France in the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> of 1803. The last serious European challenge to U.S. control of the river came at the conclusion of <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> when British forces mounted an attack on <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> – the attack <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">was repulsed</a> by an American army under the command of General <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_1818" title="Treaty of 1818">Treaty of 1818</a>, the U.S. and Great Britain agreed to fix the border running from the <a href="/wiki/Lake_of_the_Woods" title="Lake of the Woods">Lake of the Woods</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> along the <a href="/wiki/49th_parallel_north" title="49th parallel north">49th parallel north</a>. In effect, the U.S. ceded the northwestern extremity of the Mississippi basin to the British in exchange for the southern portion of the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_North" title="Red River of the North">Red River</a> basin.</p>
<p>So many settlers traveled westward through the Mississippi river basin, as well as settled in it, that Zadok Cramer wrote a guide book called <i><a href="/wiki/The_Navigator_(1801_guide_book)" title="The Navigator (1801 guide book)">The Navigator</a></i>, detailing the features and dangers and navigable waterways of the area. It was so popular that he updated and expanded it through 12 editions over a period of 25 years.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River-sand_bars.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="539" data-file-width="860" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Mississippi_River-sand_bars.jpg/220px-Mississippi_River-sand_bars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Mississippi_River-sand_bars.jpg/330px-Mississippi_River-sand_bars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Mississippi_River-sand_bars.jpg/440px-Mississippi_River-sand_bars.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River-sand_bars.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shifting <a href="/wiki/Shoal" title="Shoal">sand bars</a> made early navigation difficult.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The colonization of the area was barely slowed by the three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1812_New_Madrid_earthquake" title="1812 New Madrid earthquake">earthquakes in 1811 and 1812</a>, estimated at approximately 8 on the <a href="/wiki/Richter_magnitude_scale" title="Richter magnitude scale">Richter magnitude scale</a>, that were centered near <a href="/wiki/New_Madrid,_Missouri" title="New Madrid, Missouri">New Madrid, Missouri</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steamboat_era">Steamboat era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Steamboat era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Steamboats_of_the_Mississippi" title="Steamboats of the Mississippi">Steamboats of the Mississippi</a></div>
<p>Mark Twain's book, <i><a href="/wiki/Life_on_the_Mississippi" title="Life on the Mississippi">Life on the Mississippi</a></i>, covered the <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamboat</a> commerce which took place from 1830 to 1870 on the river before more modern ships replaced the steamer. The book was published first in serial form in <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Weekly" title="Harper's Weekly">Harper's Weekly</a></i> in seven parts in 1875. The full version, including a passage from the then unfinished <i><a href="/wiki/Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn" title="Adventures of Huckleberry Finn">Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</a></i> and works from other authors, was published by James R. Osgood &amp; Company in 1885.</p>
<p>The first steamboat to travel the full length of the Lower Mississippi from the Ohio River to New Orleans was the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Orleans_(steamboat)" title="New Orleans (steamboat)">New Orleans</a></i> in December 1811. Its maiden voyage occurred during the series of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1812_New_Madrid_earthquake" title="1812 New Madrid earthquake">New Madrid earthquakes</a> in 1811–12. The Upper Mississippi was treacherous, unpredictable and to make traveling worse, the area was not properly mapped out or surveyed. Until the 1840s only two trips a year to the Twin Cities landings were made by steamboats which suggests it was not very profitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Steamboat transport remained a viable industry, both in terms of passengers and freight until the end of the first decade of the 20th century. Among the several Mississippi River system steamboat companies was the noted <a href="/wiki/Anchor_Line_(riverboat_company)" title="Anchor Line (riverboat company)">Anchor Line</a>, which, from 1859 to 1898, operated a luxurious fleet of steamers between St. Louis and New Orleans.</p>
<p>Italian explorer Giacomo Beltrami, wrote about his journey on the Virginia, which was the first steam boat to make it to Fort St. Anthony in Minnesota. He referred to his voyage as a promenade that was once a journey on the Mississippi. The steamboat era changed the economic and political life of the Mississippi, as well as the nature of travel itself. The Mississippi was completely changed by the steamboat era as it transformed into a flourishing tourists trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War">Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Vicksburg,_Kurz_and_Allison.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="623" data-file-width="900" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Battle_of_Vicksburg%2C_Kurz_and_Allison.png/220px-Battle_of_Vicksburg%2C_Kurz_and_Allison.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Battle_of_Vicksburg%2C_Kurz_and_Allison.png/330px-Battle_of_Vicksburg%2C_Kurz_and_Allison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Battle_of_Vicksburg%2C_Kurz_and_Allison.png/440px-Battle_of_Vicksburg%2C_Kurz_and_Allison.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Vicksburg,_Kurz_and_Allison.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Vicksburg" title="Siege of Vicksburg">Battle of Vicksburg</a> (ca. 1888)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_from_Eunice,_Arkansas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Mississippi_River_from_Eunice%2C_Arkansas.jpg/220px-Mississippi_River_from_Eunice%2C_Arkansas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Mississippi_River_from_Eunice%2C_Arkansas.jpg/330px-Mississippi_River_from_Eunice%2C_Arkansas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Mississippi_River_from_Eunice%2C_Arkansas.jpg/440px-Mississippi_River_from_Eunice%2C_Arkansas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_River_from_Eunice,_Arkansas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mississippi River from <a href="/wiki/Eunice,_Arkansas" title="Eunice, Arkansas">Eunice, Arkansas</a>, a ghost town. Eunice was destroyed by gunboats during the Civil War.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Control of the river was a strategic objective of both sides in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. In 1862 <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> forces coming down the river successfully cleared Confederate defenses at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Island_Number_Ten" title="Battle of Island Number Ten">Island Number 10</a> and <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis, Tennessee</a>, while Naval forces coming upriver from the Gulf of Mexico captured <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Orleans,_Louisiana" title="New Orleans, Louisiana">New Orleans, Louisiana</a>. The remaining major Confederate stronghold was on the heights overlooking the river at <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg,_Mississippi" title="Vicksburg, Mississippi">Vicksburg, Mississippi</a>, and the Union's <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_Campaign" title="Vicksburg Campaign">Vicksburg Campaign</a> (December 1862 to July 1863), and the fall of <a href="/wiki/Port_Hudson,_Louisiana" title="Port Hudson, Louisiana">Port Hudson</a>, completed control of the lower Mississippi River. The Union victory ending the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Vicksburg" title="Siege of Vicksburg">Siege of Vicksburg</a> on July 4, 1863, was pivotal to the Union's final victory of the Civil War.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_and_21st_centuries">20th and 21st centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 20th and 21st centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_Flood_of_1927" title="Great Mississippi Flood of 1927">Great Mississippi Flood of 1927</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Flood_of_1951" title="Great Flood of 1951">Great Flood of 1951</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_and_Missouri_Rivers_Flood_of_1993" title="Great Mississippi and Missouri Rivers Flood of 1993">Great Mississippi and Missouri Rivers Flood of 1993</a></div>
<p>The "Big Freeze" of 1918–19 blocked river traffic north of Memphis, Tennessee, preventing transportation of coal from southern Illinois. This resulted in widespread shortages, high prices, and rationing of coal in January and February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the spring of 1927, the river broke out of its banks in 145 places, during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_Flood_of_1927" title="Great Mississippi Flood of 1927">Great Mississippi Flood of 1927</a> and inundated 27,000 sq mi (70,000 km<sup>2</sup>) to a depth of up to 30 feet (9.1 m).</p>
<p>In 1962 and 1963, industrial accidents spilled 3.5 million US gallons (13,000,000 L) of <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">soybean oil</a> into the Mississippi and <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_River" title="Minnesota River">Minnesota</a> rivers. The oil covered the Mississippi River from St. Paul to Lake Pepin, creating an ecological disaster and a demand to control <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 20, 1976, the automobile ferry, <i><a href="/wiki/MV_George_Prince_ferry_disaster" title="MV George Prince ferry disaster">MV George Prince</a></i>, was struck by a ship traveling upstream as the ferry attempted to cross from <a href="/wiki/Destrehan,_Louisiana" title="Destrehan, Louisiana">Destrehan, Louisiana</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Luling,_Louisiana" title="Luling, Louisiana">Luling, Louisiana</a>. Seventy-eight passengers and crew died; only eighteen survived the accident.</p>
<p>In 1988, the water level of the Mississippi fell to 10 feet (3.0 m) below zero on the Memphis gauge. The remains of wooden-hulled water craft were exposed in an area of 4.5 acres (18,000 m<sup>2</sup>) on the bottom of the Mississippi River at West Memphis, Arkansas. They dated to the late 19th to early 20th centuries. The State of Arkansas, the Arkansas Archeological Survey, and the Arkansas Archeological Society responded with a two-month data recovery effort. The fieldwork received national media attention as good news in the middle of a drought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_and_Missouri_Rivers_Flood_of_1993" title="Great Mississippi and Missouri Rivers Flood of 1993">Great Flood of 1993</a> was another significant flood, primarily affecting the Mississippi above its confluence with the Ohio River at Cairo, Illinois.</p>
<p>Two portions of the Mississippi were designated as <a href="/wiki/American_Heritage_Rivers" title="American Heritage Rivers">American Heritage Rivers</a> in 1997: the lower portion around Louisiana and Tennessee, and the upper portion around Iowa, Illinois, Minnesota and Missouri. The Nature Conservancy's project called "America's Rivershed Initiative" announced a 'report card' assessment of the entire basin in October 2015 and gave the grade of D+. The assessment noted the aging navigation and flood control infrastructure along with multiple environmental problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi-River-Sandbar-Sunset.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1181" data-file-width="1749" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Mississippi-River-Sandbar-Sunset.jpg/220px-Mississippi-River-Sandbar-Sunset.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Mississippi-River-Sandbar-Sunset.jpg/330px-Mississippi-River-Sandbar-Sunset.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Mississippi-River-Sandbar-Sunset.jpg/440px-Mississippi-River-Sandbar-Sunset.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi-River-Sandbar-Sunset.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Campsite at the river in Arkansas</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2002, <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenian</a> long-distance swimmer <a href="/wiki/Martin_Strel" title="Martin Strel">Martin Strel</a> swam the entire length of the river, from Minnesota to Louisiana, over the course of 68 days. In 2005, the Source to Sea Expedition<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> paddled the Mississippi and <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_River" title="Atchafalaya River">Atchafalaya Rivers</a> to benefit the Audubon Society's Upper Mississippi River Campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future">Future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Future">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Geologists believe that the lower Mississippi could take a new course to the Gulf. Either of two new routes—through the <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_Basin" title="Atchafalaya Basin">Atchafalaya Basin</a> or through <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pontchartrain" title="Lake Pontchartrain">Lake Pontchartrain</a>—might become the Mississippi's main channel if flood-control structures are overtopped or heavily damaged during a severe flood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmericasWetlands_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmericasWetlands-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DailyImpact_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-DailyImpact-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InteractiveMap_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-InteractiveMap-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nola_article_with_flood_volume_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nola_article_with_flood_volume-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Failure of the <a href="/wiki/Old_River_Control_Structure" title="Old River Control Structure">Old River Control Structure</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Morganza_Spillway" title="Morganza Spillway">Morganza Spillway</a>, or nearby levees would likely re-route the main channel of the Mississippi through <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_Basin" title="Atchafalaya Basin">Atchafalaya Basin</a> and down the <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_River" title="Atchafalaya River">Atchafalaya River</a> to reach the Gulf of Mexico south of <a href="/wiki/Morgan_City,_Louisiana" title="Morgan City, Louisiana">Morgan City</a> in southern Louisiana. This route provides a more direct path to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> than the present Mississippi River channel through <a href="/wiki/Baton_Rouge,_Louisiana" title="Baton Rouge, Louisiana">Baton Rouge</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InteractiveMap_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-InteractiveMap-83">[83]</a></sup> While the risk of such a diversion is present during any major flood event, such a change has so far been prevented by active human intervention involving the construction, maintenance, and operation of various levees, spillways, and other control structures by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="U.S. Army Corps of Engineers">U.S. Army Corps of Engineers</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_River_Control_Structure_Complex.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1500" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Old_River_Control_Structure_Complex.jpg/220px-Old_River_Control_Structure_Complex.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Old_River_Control_Structure_Complex.jpg/330px-Old_River_Control_Structure_Complex.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Old_River_Control_Structure_Complex.jpg/440px-Old_River_Control_Structure_Complex.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_River_Control_Structure_Complex.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Old River Control Structure complex. View is to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East-southeast" title="East-southeast">east-southeast</a>, looking downriver on the Mississippi, with the three dams across channels of the <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_River" title="Atchafalaya River">Atchafalaya River</a> to the right of the Mississippi. <a href="/wiki/Concordia_Parish,_Louisiana" title="Concordia Parish, Louisiana">Concordia Parish, Louisiana</a> is in the foreground, on the right, and <a href="/wiki/Wilkinson_County,_Mississippi" title="Wilkinson County, Mississippi">Wilkinson County, Mississippi</a>, is in the background, across the Mississippi on the left.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Old River Control Structure, between the present Mississippi River channel and the Atchafalaya Basin, sits at the normal water elevation and is ordinarily used to divert 30% of the Mississippi's flow to the Atchafalaya River. There is a steep drop here away from the Mississippi's main channel into the Atchafalaya Basin. If this facility were to fail during a major flood, there is a strong concern the water would <a href="/wiki/Bridge_scour" title="Bridge scour">scour</a> and erode the river bottom enough to capture the Mississippi's main channel. The structure was nearly lost during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1973_Mississippi_River_floods" title="1973 Mississippi River floods">1973 flood</a>, but repairs and improvements were made after engineers studied the forces at play. In particular, the Corps of Engineers made many improvements and constructed additional facilities for routing water through the vicinity. These additional facilities give the Corps much more flexibility and potential flow capacity than they had in 1973, which further reduces the risk of a catastrophic failure in this area during other major floods, such as that of <a href="/wiki/2011_Mississippi_River_floods" title="2011 Mississippi River floods">2011</a>.</p>
<p>Because the Morganza Spillway is slightly higher and well back from the river, it is normally dry on both sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Even if it failed at the crest during a severe flood, the flood waters would have to erode to normal water levels before the Mississippi could permanently jump channel at this location.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> During the 2011 floods, the Corps of Engineers opened the Morganza Spillway to 1/4 of its capacity to allow 150,000 ft<sup>3</sup>/sec of water to flood the Morganza and Atchafalaya floodways and continue directly to the Gulf of Mexico, bypassing Baton Rouge and New Orleans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usace-inundation_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-usace-inundation-87">[87]</a></sup> In addition to reducing the Mississippi River crest downstream, this diversion reduced the chances of a channel change by reducing stress on the other elements of the control system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some geologists have noted that the possibility for course change into the Atchafalaya also exists in the area immediately north of the Old River Control Structure. Army Corps of Engineers geologist Fred Smith once stated, "The Mississippi wants to go west. 1973 was a forty-year flood. The big one lies out there somewhere—when the structures can't release all the floodwaters and the levee is going to have to give way. That is when the river's going to jump its banks and try to break through."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another possible course change for the Mississippi River is a diversion into <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pontchartrain" title="Lake Pontchartrain">Lake Pontchartrain</a> near <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>. This route is controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Bonnet_Carr%C3%A9_Spillway" title="Bonnet Carré Spillway">Bonnet Carré Spillway</a>, built to reduce flooding in New Orleans. This spillway and an imperfect natural levee about 4–6 meters (12 to 20 feet) high are all that prevents the Mississippi from taking a new, shorter course through Lake Pontchartrain to the Gulf of Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Diversion of the Mississippi's main channel through Lake Pontchartrain would have consequences similar to an Atchafalaya diversion, but to a lesser extent, since the present river channel would remain in use past Baton Rouge and into the New Orleans area.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreation">Recreation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Recreation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MississippiRiverBluffs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1748" data-file-width="1178" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/MississippiRiverBluffs.jpg/170px-MississippiRiverBluffs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/MississippiRiverBluffs.jpg/255px-MississippiRiverBluffs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/MississippiRiverBluffs.jpg/340px-MississippiRiverBluffs.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MississippiRiverBluffs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Great_River_Road" title="Great River Road">Great River Road</a> in Wisconsin near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pepin" title="Lake Pepin">Lake Pepin</a> (2005)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The sport of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterskiing" title="Waterskiing">water skiing</a> was invented on the river in a wide region between Minnesota and Wisconsin known as <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pepin" title="Lake Pepin">Lake Pepin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usaws_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-usaws-91">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Samuelson" title="Ralph Samuelson">Ralph Samuelson</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lake_City,_Minnesota" title="Lake City, Minnesota">Lake City, Minnesota</a>, created and refined his skiing technique in late June and early July 1922. He later performed the first water ski jump in 1925 and was pulled along at 80 mph (130 km/h) by a Curtiss <a href="/wiki/Flying_boat" title="Flying boat">flying boat</a> later that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usaws_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-usaws-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are seven <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a> sites along the Mississippi River. The <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_National_River_and_Recreation_Area" title="Mississippi National River and Recreation Area">Mississippi National River and Recreation Area</a> is the National Park Service site dedicated to protecting and interpreting the Mississippi River itself. The other six National Park Service sites along the river are (listed from north to south):</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Effigy_Mounds_National_Monument" title="Effigy Mounds National Monument">Effigy Mounds National Monument</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jefferson_National_Expansion_Memorial" title="Jefferson National Expansion Memorial">Jefferson National Expansion Memorial</a> (includes <a href="/wiki/Gateway_Arch" title="Gateway Arch">Gateway Arch</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_National_Military_Park" title="Vicksburg National Military Park">Vicksburg National Military Park</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Natchez_National_Historical_Park" title="Natchez National Historical Park">Natchez National Historical Park</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/New_Orleans_Jazz_National_Historical_Park" title="New Orleans Jazz National Historical Park">New Orleans Jazz National Historical Park</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jean_Lafitte_National_Historical_Park_and_Preserve" title="Jean Lafitte National Historical Park and Preserve">Jean Lafitte National Historical Park and Preserve</a></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paddlefish_underwater.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="469" data-file-width="590" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f7/Paddlefish_underwater.jpeg/220px-Paddlefish_underwater.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f7/Paddlefish_underwater.jpeg/330px-Paddlefish_underwater.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f7/Paddlefish_underwater.jpeg/440px-Paddlefish_underwater.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paddlefish_underwater.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/American_paddlefish" title="American paddlefish">American paddlefish</a> is an ancient <a href="/wiki/Relict_(biology)" title="Relict (biology)">relict</a> from the Mississippi</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Mississippi basin is home to a highly diverse aquatic <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a> and has been called the "mother fauna" of North American fresh water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews1998_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews1998-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish">Fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>About 375 fish species are known from the Mississippi basin, far exceeding other North Hemisphere river basin exclusively within temperate/subtropical regions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews1998_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews1998-92">[92]</a></sup> except the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Within the Mississippi basin, streams that have their source in the Appalachian and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozark" title="Ozark">Ozark</a> highlands contain especially many species. Among the fish species in the basin are numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic" title="Endemic">endemics</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Relict_(biology)" title="Relict (biology)">relicts</a> such as <a href="/wiki/American_paddlefish" title="American paddlefish">paddlefish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sturgeon" title="Sturgeon">sturgeon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gar" title="Gar">gar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bowfin" title="Bowfin">bowfin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews1998_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews1998-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because of its size and high species diversity, the Mississippi basin is often divided into subregions. The Upper Mississippi River alone is home to about 120 fish species, including <a href="/wiki/Walleye" title="Walleye">walleye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sauger" title="Sauger">sauger</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large_mouth_bass" title="Large mouth bass">large mouth bass</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_mouth_bass" title="Small mouth bass">small mouth bass</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_bass" title="White bass">white bass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_pike" title="Northern pike">northern pike</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bluegill" title="Bluegill">bluegill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crappie" title="Crappie">crappie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Channel_catfish" title="Channel catfish">channel catfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flathead_catfish" title="Flathead catfish">flathead catfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_shiner" title="Common shiner">common shiner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_drum" title="Freshwater drum">freshwater drum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shovelnose_sturgeon" title="Shovelnose sturgeon">shovelnose sturgeon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_fauna">Other fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Other fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to fish, several species of turtles (such as <a href="/wiki/Common_snapping_turtle" title="Common snapping turtle">snapping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sternotherus" title="Sternotherus">musk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kinosternon" title="Kinosternon">mud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graptemys" title="Graptemys">map</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pseudemys" title="Pseudemys">cooter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Painted_turtle" title="Painted turtle">painted</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apalone" title="Apalone">softshell</a> turtles), <a href="/wiki/American_alligator" title="American alligator">American alligator</a>, aquatic amphibians (such as <a href="/wiki/Hellbender" title="Hellbender">hellbender</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mudpuppy" title="Mudpuppy">mudpuppy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three-toed_amphiuma" title="Three-toed amphiuma">three-toed amphiuma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lesser_siren" title="Lesser siren">lesser siren</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cambaridae" title="Cambaridae">cambarid</a> crayfish (such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_swamp_crayfish" title="Red swamp crayfish">red swamp crayfish</a>) are native to the Mississippi basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduced_species">Introduced species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Introduced species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Numerous <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced species</a> are found in the Mississippi and some of these are <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive</a>. Among the introductions are fish such as <a href="/wiki/Asian_carp_in_North_America" title="Asian carp in North America">Asian carp</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Silver_carp" title="Silver carp">silver carp</a> that have become infamous for outcompeting native fish and their potentially dangerous <a href="/wiki/Asian_carp_in_North_America#Jumping_ability" title="Asian carp in North America">jumping behavior</a>. They have spread throughout much of the basin, even approaching (but not yet invading) the Great Lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_Department_of_Natural_Resources" title="Minnesota Department of Natural Resources">Minnesota Department of Natural Resources</a> has designated much of the Mississippi River in the state as infested waters by the exotic species <a href="/wiki/Zebra_mussel" title="Zebra mussel">zebra mussels</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurasian_watermilfoil" title="Eurasian watermilfoil">Eurasian watermilfoil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_references">Cultural references</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Cultural references">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
