<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_Faulkner" title="William Faulkner">William Faulkner</a> uses the Mississippi River and Delta as the setting for many hunts throughout his novels. It has been proposed that in Faulkner's famous story, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_Down,_Moses" title="Go Down, Moses">The Bear</a></i>, young Ike first begins his transformation into a man, thus relinquishing his birthright to land in <a href="/wiki/Yoknapatawpha_County" title="Yoknapatawpha County">Yoknapatawpha County</a> through his realizations found within the woods surrounding the Mississippi River.</li>
<li>Many of the works of Mark Twain deal with or take place near the Mississippi River. One of his first major works, <i><a href="/wiki/Life_on_the_Mississippi" title="Life on the Mississippi">Life on the Mississippi</a></i>, is in part a history of the river, in part a memoir of Twain's experiences on the river, and a collection of tales that either take place on or are associated with the river. The river was noted for the number of bandits which called its islands and shores home, including <a href="/wiki/John_Murrell_(bandit)" title="John Murrell (bandit)">John Murrell</a> who was a well-known murderer, horse stealer and slave "re-trader". His notoriety was such that author Twain devoted an entire chapter to him in <i>Life on the Mississippi</i>, and Murrell was rumored to have an island headquarters on the river at Island 37. Twain's most famous work, <i><a href="/wiki/Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn" title="Adventures of Huckleberry Finn">Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</a></i>, is largely a journey down the river. The novel works as an episodic meditation on American culture with the river having multiple different meanings including independence, escape, freedom, and adventure.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Herman_Melville" title="Herman Melville">Herman Melville</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Confidence-Man" title="The Confidence-Man">The Confidence-Man</a></i> portrayed a <a href="/wiki/The_Canterbury_Tales" title="The Canterbury Tales"><i>Canterbury Tales</i></a>-style group of steamboat passengers whose interlocking stories are told as they travel down the Mississippi River. The novel is written both as cultural satire and a metaphysical treatise. Like <i>Huckleberry Finn</i>, it uses the Mississippi River as a metaphor for the larger aspects of American and human identity that unify the otherwise disparate characters. The river's fluidity is reflected by the often shifting personalities and identities of Melville's "confidence man".</li>
<li>Much of <a href="/wiki/Edna_Ferber" title="Edna Ferber">Edna Ferber</a>'s 1926 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Show_Boat_(novel)" title="Show Boat (novel)">Show Boat</a></i> takes place on the Mississippi River. The novel is the basis for the celebrated <a href="/wiki/Show_Boat" title="Show Boat">1927 musical play</a> by <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Kern" title="Jerome Kern">Jerome Kern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Hammerstein_II" title="Oscar Hammerstein II">Oscar Hammerstein II</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:On_The_Mississippi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="241" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/On_The_Mississippi.jpg/170px-On_The_Mississippi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/On_The_Mississippi.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:On_The_Mississippi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>On The Mississippi</i>, music sheet cover for a 1912 song</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>The song "<a href="/wiki/When_the_Levee_Breaks" title="When the Levee Breaks">When the Levee Breaks</a>", made famous in the version performed by <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a> on the album <i><a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin_IV" title="Led Zeppelin IV">Led Zeppelin IV</a></i>, was composed by <a href="/wiki/Memphis_Minnie" title="Memphis Minnie">Memphis Minnie</a> McCoy in 1929 after the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_Flood_of_1927" title="Great Mississippi Flood of 1927">Great Mississippi Flood of 1927</a>. Another song about the flood was "Louisiana 1927" by <a href="/wiki/Randy_Newman" title="Randy Newman">Randy Newman</a> for the album <i>Good Old Boys.</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ferde_Grof%C3%A9" title="Ferde Grofé">Ferde Grofé</a> composed a set of movements for symphony orchestra entitled "<a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Suite" title="Mississippi Suite">Mississippi Suite</a>", based on the lands the river travels through.</li>
<li>The stage and movie musical <i><a href="/wiki/Show_Boat" title="Show Boat">Show Boat</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s central musical piece is the <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a>-influenced ballad "<a href="/wiki/Ol%27_Man_River" title="Ol' Man River">Ol' Man River</a>". Its composer, <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Kern" title="Jerome Kern">Jerome Kern</a>, also composed an orchestral piece entitled "Mark Twain Suite".</li>
<li>The musical <i><a href="/wiki/Big_River_(musical)" title="Big River (musical)">Big River</a></i> is based on the travels of Huckleberry Finn down the river.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Cash" title="Johnny Cash">Johnny Cash</a> song "<a href="/wiki/Big_River_(Johnny_Cash_song)" title="Big River (Johnny Cash song)">Big River</a>" is about the Mississippi River, and about drifting the length of the river to pursue a relationship that fails. The places mentioned in the song are <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul,_Minnesota" title="Saint Paul, Minnesota">Saint Paul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Davenport,_Iowa" title="Davenport, Iowa">Davenport</a>, <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memphis" title="Memphis">Memphis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baton_Rouge" title="Baton Rouge">Baton Rouge</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>.</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Louisiana_1927" title="Louisiana 1927">Louisiana 1927</a>" is a 1974 song written and recorded by Randy Newman on the album Good Old Boys. It tells the story of the Great Mississippi River Flood of 1927 which left 700,000 people homeless in Louisiana and Mississippi.</li>
<li>Illinois-born singer <a href="/wiki/Lissie" title="Lissie">Lissie</a> has a song called <i>Oh Mississippi</i> dedicated to the river.</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Roll_On_Mississippi" title="Roll On Mississippi">Roll On Mississippi</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Cotton_Picking_Delta_Town" title="Mississippi Cotton Picking Delta Town">Mississippi Cotton Picking Delta Town</a>" are two classics from <a href="/wiki/Charley_Pride" title="Charley Pride">Charley Pride</a> that refer to the Mississippi River.</li>
<li>The late <a href="/wiki/Conway_Twitty" title="Conway Twitty">Conway Twitty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Loretta_Lynn" title="Loretta Lynn">Loretta Lynn</a> collaborated on the song "<a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Woman,_Mississippi_Man" title="Louisiana Woman, Mississippi Man">Louisiana Woman, Mississippi Man</a>".</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paul_Simon" title="Paul Simon">Paul Simon</a> mentions the river and the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Delta" title="Mississippi Delta">Mississippi Delta</a> in his song "<a href="/wiki/Graceland_(song)" title="Graceland (song)">Graceland</a>".</li>
</ul>

The other definition acknowledges "somewhat arbitrary decisions" and places the Mississippi's source at Lake Itasca, which is publicly accepted as the source,<a class="external text" href="http://pubs.usgs.gov/of/1987/ofr87-242/" rel="nofollow">USGS.gov</a> and which had been identified as such by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_V._Brower" title="Jacob V. <li>Carlander, Harriet Bell. <i>A history of fish and fishing in the upper Mississippi River</i> (PhD Diss. Iowa State College, 1954) <a class="external text" href="http://www.nativefishlab.net/library/textpdf/17391.pdf" rel="nofollow">online</a> (PDF)</li>
<li>Daniel, Pete. <i>Deep'n as it come: The 1927 Mississippi River flood</i> (University of Arkansas Press, 1977)</li>
<li>Fremling, Calvin R. <i>Immortal river: the Upper Mississippi in ancient and modern times</i> (U. of Wisconsin Press, 2005), popular history</li>
<li>Milner, George R. "The late prehistoric Cahokia cultural system of the Mississippi River valley: Foundations, florescence, and fragmentation." <i>Journal of World Prehistory</i> (1990) 4#1 pp: 1–43.</li>
<li>Morris, Christopher. <i>The Big Muddy: An Environmental History of the Mississippi and Its Peoples From Hernando de Soto to Hurricane Katrina</i> (Oxford University Press; 2012) 300 pages; links drought, disease, and flooding to the impact of centuries of increasingly intense human manipulation of the river.</li>
